Contemporary Science.
The development of the theory of evolution, that grand conception which unites
all phenomena m one splendid synthesis under uniform law, remains the most attrac-
tive feature of contemporary science. From year to year the hypothesis is altered
in its details but its general truth becomes always clearer, and those who now reject
it do so only by impugning the quality of their intellect. It is not as if there were
rival theories of defensible validity. All the facts point one way. For creation or
emanation there are assumptions and presumptions innumerable, but not a vestige
of ponderable proof. For evolution there is all the proof that is. Its chain of evi-
dence may not be complete ; the links that are wanting have probably been lost for
e\er, but sufficient remain to amply justify the general judgment of scientists.
Sporadic objectors, like Lord Salisbury, doubtfully suggesting the argument for
design to the consideration of the British Association ; or Mrs. Besant, confidently
comparing the weakness of inductive logic with the sweet reasonableness sweated
rom the swoons of a Master-Mahatma, are mere retrograde ripples swallowed in the
advancing tide of thought. Lord Salisbury’s cavil that physicists and biologists
cannot agree as to time available and time demanded for the processes of
evolution, gives ground for believing that either physicists or biologists, or
both, are m error; but affords no basis for postulating supernatural inter-
ference with observed uniformity of natural law. Mrs. Besant’s quotation of
Weismann a " de “7 ln g perpetuation of acquired structural variations, and of
Huxley as admitting that ethical advance is opposed to the scheme of natural
selection, is a pleasant irrelevancy of the platform. Neither Weismann nor Huxley,
as Mrs. Besant might add at future lectures, has the least idea that the evolu-
tionary theory is weakened by their admissions. It is only modified. Weismann
simply shifts the load from natural selection to sexual selection; and Huxley, while
re-stating the truism that altruism is opposed to egoism, recognises as a matter of
course that both altruism and egoism join rhythmically in evolutionary unity.
The tendency of current thought is to show that the individual is in the plan
of existence as nothing compared with the race—that the most complex individual
lives are, in Fosters phrase, “ but the bye-play of ovum-bearing organisms.” Here
again evolution comes directly into conflict with Oriental creeds which lay stress on
the need for purification of the Ego by a short or long course of trial, with a view
to final salvation. More and more clearly is it to be seen that this view is un-
scientific and unwarrantable—the dear delusion of anthropocentric myths which
measure the Atom with the All, and hail him master of a universe where his function
is solely that of a life-bearer, and his identity is a matter of the profoundest cosmic
indifference.
In the “Contemporary Science Series,” a list of which appears below, may be
found the most recent and readable books on evolution, as on many other minor
phases of scientific struggle. Such works as Drummond’s “Ascent of Man ” and
Kidds Social Evolution,” written to bolster preconceived theories, cannot compare
m interest and value with a book like “The Evolution of Sex,” a most masterly
review of a wide and fascinating field. Weismann’s “Germ-Plasm” is strong meat
for begmners, and wants some previous training to be appreciated. Lombroso’s
•Man of Genius and Ellis’s “Criminal” and “Man and Woman” (elsewhere
noted) have special charm for average readers; but the whole series can be warmly
recommended. Posted for 3s. 9d. each, with exceptions noted.

A party of Condobolin shearers accom-
panied a mate, suffering from toothache, into a
saloon where teeth are pulled. When the colored
gemman who operates began work the shearer
howled loudly, whereupon Bill the Ringer ; “You
are a cocktail ! Just stop quiet a minute and
this man will soon fix you up. He’s a first-class
hand at drorin’ teeth. He’s drawn many a
hundred of mine.” And then (reflectively): “My
gory oath ! I haven’t hardly any left.”
i Labouchere professes to be unable to
understand how Robert Buchanan, a moralist
earning .£l5OO a year, can fall so heavily as to
have for his only assets in insolvency a few copy-
| rights. Labby and Buchanan have never made
■ up the historic squabble in which the former,
drenched with 17 vials of vituperation, remarked
that “ Mr. Buchanan came to me a hungry and
scrofulous Scotchman. I gave him meat for his
belly and sulphur for his back; and this is my
reward !”

A Monaro man has gained the belt as
champion gormandizer. One of his latest essays
was the consumption of 16 eggs, most of them
goose and turkey eggs, also a couple of loaves of
bread and three quarts of tea. He could have
eaten more but was cut short by the culinary
official, who objected on purely economic grounds.
It is unknown what amount of food he is capable
■ of devouring, as he has never been given a fair go ;
however, his admirers are so sanguine that he is
| invincible they are prepared to back their
“ gorger ” to consume more food, at a sitting, than
any man in Australia.
The hospital on one mining field was run
a bit differently in the early Seventies from what
it is now. There were only visiting-surgeons then,
the warder being the sole resident official. That
was Johnny B. Johnny mostly used to be “down
town ” on an afternoon and evening, and was
generally “tanglefooted ” when he got back. One
night he was in a particularly dogmatic humour,
and, as he went his rounds, made some comforting
remark to each patient. Presently he came to
one whose early demise was a certainty. “Well,”
said Johnny, reflectively, “ not dead yet! Why,
in the Yale of Hinnom, can’t you—hie—die
decently in the daytime ? Here you’ll go slippin’
it in the' middle of the night and in the mornin’
it’ll be—hie —perfect Sheol to straighten you out!”

N.S.W. Premier Reid’s pronouncement of
January last that “Single-tax is robbery ” stands
like a rod in pickle and will fall on his back at an
early date.
A commercial estimate of the respective
benefits derived or to be derived by N.S.W. from
the Philadelphia and Chicago Exhibitions places
the former in the front. Yet Philadelphia cost
N.S.W. ,£3000 only, whereas Chicago stood her
£28,924.
Lamentation of a N.S. ■/. political Jere-
miah : —“As long as I wore the garb of an Ishmael
and proclaimed myself a vagabond my constituents
sent me into Parliament; but as soon as I took a
regular bath and endeavoured to be a respectable
member of society they kicked me out.”
The labor-vessel May touched lately at
Tucopra, an island 150 miles east of Santa Cruz.
As neither traders nor missionaries had hitherto
touched there the people were fairly prosperous,
peaceable and increasing in numbers. The captain
reports that he found there a race of giants, 6ft.
high with hair about 18in. long. He asserts also
that the natives actually didn't want to go to
Queensland. The latter statement, however,
gives away the captain and brands him as a
Munchausen of the first water.
St. James’ Gazette (London) publishes
a beautiful photo, of the cow on whose milk the
York baby is being fed. Presumably the pro-
prietors of the crawlsome print understand the
tastes of their degraded customers, and know that
a picture of the cow on whose milk the York baby
is fed will be a matter of interest, and will help to
boom the sale of the paper. Moreover, the por-
trait of the cow helps to maintain the spirit of
loyalty, and loyalty is the silken bond which
keeps the empire together. Therefore, the Gazette,
as a loyal publication, is wise in its crawling day
and generation, for in these revolutionary times
the royal fetish wants all the support it can get,
and even an engraving of the lank and dismal
bovine which furnishes nutriment to Victoria’s
toothless descendant is a buttress to the Throne
and another barrier against the inroads of Demo-
cracy.

The policeman’s foot, it seems, is not a
joke, after all, but a scientific reality. An Eng-
lish journalist who has interviewed sundry police-
men on the subject reports : —
It is a fact —but heretofore unohronicled —that the size
of a policeman’s foot increases according to his time of
service. A policeman who wears a No. 9 shoe when he
goes on the force has to replace it with a No. 10 after a
year’s pedestrian exercise, with a No. 10J or 11 after two
years, and with a 11J or 12 after three years' active em-
ployment. Just why this is true is a matter for scientific
investigation, worthy of a special article for one of the
heavy monthly periodicals and entirely too abstruse for
consideration’in a sketch designed for newspaper
readers. Whether constant walking has a tendency to
spread out the foot or not must be determined by some
specialist in pedal extremities, whose attention is here-
with directed to a new field of theoretical research. It is
sufficient for the present that policemen, as a rule, do not
have shapely feet. After a few months’ service the aris-
tocratic high instep disappears, the arch is annihilated,
and that tapering toward the toes which is so effective
when the foot is shod in a patent-leather shoe is gone for-
ever. The foot becomes as flat as a pancake, and tracks
left in the mud by an old time policeman look like the
footprints of a full grown elephant.
The successful removal of Piles of the
most stubborn description is being effected by
Sharp’s new “Cannalin” treatment, large quan-
tities of which are being forwarded to sufferers
throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with
advice, from 257 George-street, Sydney.
“ Virtue is its own reward ’’—old maiden-
hood.

By sending direct to Tub Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required,
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have Tub Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city Tub Bulletin is not delivered by mail , but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.

to be addressed to William Maclbod, Managing Director

tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
ParayiajJis, Letters, or News paper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
Tub Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But ue desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission oj such MSS.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 29, 1894.
The Absentee Problem.
Australia is, above all other countries,
civilised and uncivilised, the land of the
absentee. He dates almost from the first
beginnings of its history, and has multiplied
with dreadful persistence ever since. He is
a curse which accumulates, and is still accu-
mulating, at compound interest. The trou-
ble began in the early days with the granting
of large tracts of land to influential settlers,
either gratis or for merely nominal prices,
with a view to creating what was termed an
“influx of population.” The experiment
looked sufficiently feasible, but the end of it
was disastrous. The owners of most of
the large giants which still remain intact,
or nearly intact, now reside in Eng-
land. Their lands have been made valuable
by the growth of a community which they
did little or nothing to assist and encourage;
and because of this Australia pays to them
in rent—which rent is expended in England
—about two thousand per cent, per
annum on their original investment. Other
grants have been sold at several dozen—
or several hundred times their first
cost (assuming that there was any first
cost, which was not always the case); in other
words, Australia bought back, at an enor-
mous price, what it had originally given
away almost for nothing. Sometimes the
seller took the money to England ; some-
times he invested it in Australia and it piled
up unearned increment and brought in—and
still brings in—steadily increasing rentals
thatgo the way of most of Australia’s wealth—-
towards England. This country’s account
with a good many of its grand old pioneers
stands thus : It gave them, or sold them for
a few hundred pounds, a vast estate which
they did practically nothing to improve. It
made the estate valuable, and because of this
it had to buy back the property for many
thousands. And then the thousands were
invested in urban lands, and a city was built
up round them, and they multiplied in value,
and Australia pays thousands annually now,
and will pay more thousands annually in the
future, and is pledged to keep on paying
them for ever and ever, to a British absentee,
merely because, years ago, he was a forlorn
stranger and this country took him in.
* * *
When the gold-era came the absentee
curse became great and terrible. An im-
mense percentage of the gold—that part of
it which probably did Australia the least
harm—was carried away to England almost
as soon as found. A lot more remained as
the invested capital of absentees. Victoria,
during its brief history, has produced nearly
£240,000,000 worth of the yellow metal, and
yet the whole wealth of the province, apart
from its unimproved land values—everything
that its population has accumulated in the
shape of buildings, improvements of all
kinds, portable property of every description,
shipping, railways, and the like—hardly
amounts to more than £340,000,000. And
these assets are mortgaged to England for
over £100,000,000, reckoning public and
private liabilities. So that the whole wealth
of Victoria hardly equals what it has gained
from the gold industry alone ; for fifty years
of patient toil in pastoral, agricultural and
manufacturing pursuits there is nothing what-
ever to show, for the absentee has taken it all.
And the experience of Victoria has been, in a
greater or less degree, the experience of all
Australia. The story of one typical pioneer
is worth quoting here by way of illustration.
In the latter “ fifties ” a penniless English
emigrant landed in Victoria, and by good
luck made, within a year, a reasonable for-
tune among the gullies of Bendigo. Unlike
most of the lucky pioneers who survived the
rum and frenzy of these days, he did not
carry his entire fortune away with him, but
invested a small part of it in a block of land
on what was then the outskirts of Melbourne.
Then he went “ Home ” and never returned,
but without his assistance the city grew and
swallowed up his patch of soil, and it was let
on building-leases which were renewed at
steadily increasing rentals, until Victoria
now pays him, or his descendants or repre-
sentatives, some £25,000 a year, merely
because he troubled the country with his
presence for a few months some 40 years
ago. By-and-bye it will pay him £50,000 a
year ; then, later on, the burden will in-
crease to £lOO,OOO, and will still keep on
increasing. Thus the evil which he, and all
others of his kind, did lives after them, and
Australia must keep for ever paying interest
on its own wealth, till at last its gold makes
it the poorest country on this earth.
• • •
The same curse is still working. The men
who made great fortunes out of Mt. Morgan
and Broken Hill have mostly gone to spend
them in England. Some of them took their
pile with them ; others invested much of it
in Australia, and this country will pay interest
on its own gold and silver until it is paid
and sent to England twenty times over, and
yet is still owing, and must be paid twenty
or a hundred times more. The men who
are making great fortunes on the Westralian
goldfields will probably go to England in
their turn. When they go they will most
likely own among them a very large pro-
portion of the lands on which the towns of
the new goldfields are built, and because of
the gold which is dug out of the earth and
mostly sent to England these towns will
increase, and rents will go up enormously,
and a large percentage of the rents will like-
wise go to the absentee-owners in England,
till at last Coolgardie and the Murchison
make Australia poorer than it was before,
just as many other goldfields have done.
• • «
Even this, however, is only the outer
fringe of the evil. A little while ago
the Westralian Government sold a good-
sized province to an English company,
the famous Hampton Plains syndicate,
at 2s. 6d. an acre, and gave it also the
option of buying one or two more provinces
at the same rate. The land has since proved
to be auriferous, and Australia will, in the
future, probably have to pay rent to another
incorporated British absentee, at the rate of
a hundred, or several hundreds, per cent,
per annum. Sixty years ago an even worse
monopoly was created in South Australia,
and endures unto this day. Sundry bene-
volent English gentlemen obtained a grant
of many fertile miles of land in that pro-
vince, under the solemn assurance that their
object was solely to encourage settlement.
South Australia has paid, since then,
something like a million-and-a half in
rentals to these same kindly absentees
—who are now famous as about the hardest
rack-renters in the province—and has no
prospect of getting rid of them through all
futurity. The land - grant railways now
building in W.A., and those to be built in
Queensland, will create more absentee land-
lords, and how much they will ultimately
cost in rentals—all of which must go to
England—is beyond estimate, for the possi-
bilities are all but illimitable. When the
Colo Valley electric-lighting monopoly is
confirmed by New South Wales Parliament,
it will also be floated in London, and then
the money which Sydney and the surround-
ing towns pay for lighting and motive power
will likewise go to the inevitable country
from which none of Australia’s legal tender
ever returns. Everything that is valuable in
Australia goes the same road, and gets,
sooner or later, to the same destination.
* * *
Of the so-called Australian banks four are
English institutions. These rendered Aus-
tralia the questionable benefit of introducing,
years ago, a certain amount of capital, and
have ground out of this country dividends
ranging from six to thirty per cent, in
return. The capital introduced has already
been repaid probably twice over, and it will
go on being repaid and still being owed
indefinitely. The shares of most of the
purely Australian banks—as they are com-
monly described—are also largely held in
London, and they also are, to a great extent,
mere machines for grinding out dividends
for British capitalits. About two dozen
English loan, mortgage and finance com-
panies trade in Australia, and carry away
some hundreds of thousands annually
to pay divdends in England. Nearly
'fifty English fire insurance companies
likewise infest this country, and take
out of it something like £lOO,OOO per
annum in profits, giving nothing in return.
Many millions of British capital have come
here in the shape of deposits and debenture
money, and the interest which they earn also
departs never to be seen again. Many of the
big importing firms are merely agencies for
British houses ; the profits they realise go to
England, and the deluded inhabitants of
New South Wales adhere to a Freetrade
policy, in order that these profits may not be
diminished or unduly interfered with by the
competition of local manufacturers. And, as
the result of it all, Australasia—which, by
reason of its gold and silver and its great
areas of virgin soil, should have been the
richest land on earth—has always been so
poor that nearly all the money necessary for
railway construction and other public works
had to be borrowed—much of it at exorbi-
tant interest—from the same universal
pawnbroker. The seven provinces now
owe the British money-lender about
£210,000,000 of public loans, and some-
where approaching £300,000,000 in debts
due to private creditors and miscel-
laneous absentees. The total annual
tribute which Australia pays to the British
usurer, for interest on money lent, rental of
lands exploited, and profits to commercial
adventurers, is unascertainable, but it is pro-
bably not less than £20,000,000 annually, or
about £5 per year for each inhabitant.
# # •
The public debt of over two hundred mil-
lions may fairly be charged, in part, at least,
to extravagance and incapacity, but it alone
is a burden which could be borne with
safety and comparative ease. The real causes
of this country’s impoverishment are the
British commercial exploiters, company-pro-
moters, syndicators, and private money-
lenders, the British adventurers who made
fortunes in Australia, and invested them at
usury and then went “Home” to be a
burden on this country for ever afterwards,
and the unpatriotic Australians who draw
rents and dividends out of their native land
and spend them in Europe and elsewhere.
No other State has grown up under like con-
ditions, for the art of finance, in its com-
plete and beautiful developments, is a thing
of very recent date, and Australia, as the
latest born of the nations, is the only one
which has had the shadow of foreign usury
over it from its cradle. Therefore there is
no exactly similar case in history to show
what the end is to be. But, by all present
appearances the end —unless some kind of
legislation can be devised for the extinction
of absenteeism in all its worst forms—must
be insolvency. The tribute which Australia
pays to its multitudinous British creditors is
as utterly lost to the community as if it were
annually sunk in the ocean. The country
does not raise enough surplus gold to pay
interest on the universal mortgage, so it
lives mainly on the influx of more and still
more foreign capital—in other words, it
lives by adding to its burdens and making
its condition worse than it was before. No
Australasian Government, save that of Maori-
land, has even attempted to face the pro-
blem, and Maoriland is apparently beginning
to discover that the problem is almost too
much for it. Australia will presently make
the same discovery, and, after that .
Annie Besant: a Theory.
To many people Annie Besant is a puzzle, a
paradox. They contrast the creed she has left
with the creed she has joined, neo-Materialism
with neo-Theosophy ; and they see that the two
are absolutely antagonistic, mutually exclusive.
Yet here is a woman who passes from one to the
other “ somewhat suddenly,” in Bradlauqh’s
weighed and guarded phrase ; almost without a
struggle, as it appears to others. In a moment
she literally turns her mind upside-down,
astounding friends by the ease with which she
quits long-cherished convictions, and becoming
immediately no ■ less ardent and obstinate a
champion of her new faith than she was of her
old. The fruits of 20 years of strenuous thought
melt in a single glance from the “brilliant
eyes” of Madame Blavatsky. Admitting
her honesty, her sanity, how possibly
account for a revolution so radical ? But
consider. The very violence of the contradic-
tion between Annie Besant the Materialist and
Annie Besant the Theosophist implies a close
bond of unity. For it is of the essence of
things that likeness breeds opposition, unlike-
ness apposition. Extremes meet, complexity
is nearest simplicity ; and the universe rings
with the chime of contraries. Perchance our
paradox may sit on the inmost verge of
harmony.
• # •
This much is certain : the factors of the
problem are simply the every-day factors of
action and re action between organism and
environment. In the mental world, as i Q
the physical, there is no escape from
the scientific law of necessity. Effect every,
where follows cause; and with a sufficient
knowledge of principles and conditions we can
easily trace either for the finite distance corres-
ponding with finite capacity. Mrs. Besant
acted as she did act simply because, being what
she was, subject to the particular set of i n .
fluences to which she was subject, she could
not possibly act otherwise. The reasons f o p
her conduct lie firstly in herself, secondly ffi
her circumstances; and there is no room f or
any other reasons whatever. Study of her lif Q
should furnish the key to her character. Now
her mental life has experienced two great
crises: her conversion from Materialism to
Theosophy, and her previous conversion from
Christianity to Materialism. Mrs. Besant’s
autobiography and other sources of information
yield a wide induction of facts; and, applyi n „
deductively conclusions gained in other fields
of enquiry, a theory leaps at once to light. The
argument naturally takes syllogistic form.
* * #
Here is the major. The bent of man is
intellectual; the bent of woman is emotional.
This is a familiar philosophic statement justified
by a large number of special sex-observations
and supported by common experience. E x .
ceptions are sufficiently explained on the ground
of hermaphroditic race-ancestry; and Weis-
mann, the German naturalist whose thought
and experiments are doing so much to mould
current science, believes that the elements of
both sexes are in every individual, the one class
active, the other passive. Occasionally the
predominance is not marked, and primary
characters of one sex accompany secondary
characters of the other in the same individual.
Naturally, sex-generalisations apply particularly
to those individuals in whom sex is most pro-
minent, and in a less degree to those gathered
closer to the median line of difference. But,
generally speaking, though in savagery the
sexes seem almost equally superstitious, as
civilisation advances the domain of faith becomes
more and more clearly that of woman, and the
domain of reason that of man. Women are every-
where the willing supporters of clericalism,
long after men have emancipated themselves
from its sway. They judge intuitively rather
than rationally, arguing to what they wish to
be rather than from what is. In popular
language, they are ruled, not by their brains,
but by their hearts.
* * *
Annie Besant is essentially a womanly
woman. She would not wear men’s clothes,
like George Sand ; or in her own person defy
conventional morality, like George Eliot with
Lewes. (The defence of the Knowlton pamph-
let was a matter of principle which her feminine
conscience justified.) Mrs. Besant’s muliebrity
is shown in a hundred things which we are
used to consider manifestations of sex. She
has been wife and mother ; she is constitution-
ally anabolic, with rounded contours and small
bones, needing slight nourishment; she is not
above little coquetries of dressor little vanities of
nature —as, for instance, in being always photo-
graphed full-face because her profile is not
effective ; “ her voice was ever soft, gentle, and
low—an excellent thing in woman ; ” she is
tenacious of life—she suffered more and longer
than Bradlaugh, yet Bradlaugh is dead and
she is alive and well, probably partly because
he was man and she woman ; she is an inter-
preter, not a creator; she has the feminine
quality of receptivity in a very high degree,
and the masculine quality of originality in a
very low degree—Constance Naden, who had
both, died young, and Mrs. Besant should
live to be 90, though nothing she has written
will remain, and some things that Miss Naden
w'rote are imperishable, for of such is the law of
compensations. Add to this that, throughout
her life—as a school-girl, praying to a sensuous
Christ ; as a mother, fighting with Death for
her child ; as a friend, faithful to the end to
Charles Bradlaugh ; as a thinker, yearning
for some fixed basis of faith, some definite hope
of immortality—Mrs. Besant has exemplified
always the altruistic woman, a vessel of love
and devotion, never the egoistic man ; and our
minor premiss may fairly stand as established.
* 9 *
The conclusion comes of itself. Mrs. Besant
is ruled by her emotions, not by her intellect,
keen though it be. Her 16 years of Materialism
were 16 years of error, 16 years of wandering
from her proper path, 16 years in which her
real nature lay fettered and dormant. Only
now is she on her true line, is she herself. The
mystic promises of Theosophy satisfy her
latent longings as the hard facts of Materialism
never could satisfy them. Her intellect protests
feebly, but is over-ruled. She admits that her
belief in Theosophy is only partly a matter of in-
tellectual conviction. Nevertheless, she believes
wholly, because belief is a necessity of her nature.
All similar emotional characters make a similar
choice. No half-measures are possible to them.
Cardinal Newman, confronted with the dilemma,
“ Absolute Rome or absolute reason,” after an
agonised struggle chose Rome, because his
emotions dominated his intellect. His brother
chose reason, because his intellect dominated
his emotions. Once made, the characteristic
choice is final; and, as in both Cardinal New-
man and Annie Besant, the intellect is set to
work to justify the dogmas against which it
fought. The process is curious, but perfectly
intelligible ; since it is essential to the peace of
the ruling mental force-majority that the
demon Doubt be excluded from the citadel of
the Ego.
m m m
In Mrs. Besant’s case, the years which the
materialistic locust hath eaten are easily
accounted for. On the threshold of woman-
hood she made a most unhappy marriage
with a clergyman who treated her cruelly,
the consequences of which, in mental distress
and physical illness which all but killed her,
accentuated her previous sense of the weakness
of the Christian religion, and for a time com-
pletely subverted her nature. Had she married
a man whom she could love and respect, she
would have merged her views in his, sacrificed
her individuality to his welfare, and lost her
cavils in the glow of a deeper, but still


orthodox faith. It is directly to her misery
that wo owe Mrs. Besant’s magnificent life-
work. The tortured heart re-acted through
the burning brain. Yet the eclipse of her
inner self would have been but temporary had
she not met Charles Bradlaugh. His in-
fluence confirmed the new bias so decisively
that long years passed before it was gradually
lost. Only when Mrs. Besant had passed
middle age did she begin to feel lonely in her
cold, unconsolatory atheism. The longing for
human sympathy, besetting almost all men and
women after youth has vanished, became in her
an irresistible craving which mere philanthropic
work was unable to satisfy. Her heart regained
its supremacy, and cried loudly for a warmer
faith. Comte’s “ religion of humanity ” could
not suffice a character abhorrent of compromise.
From unbelief she passed at a bound to belief.
Yet her conversion to Theosophy, though it
appeared sudden, was not the expression of a
sudden change. It was preceded by a growing
conviction that the materialistic philosophy
was insufficient, and by the significant espousal
of Socialism —as compared with Individualism,
a more altruistic, more feminine, more emo-
tional creed. And at last Annie Besant’s life-
stream, long diverted, took its natural course.
• • «
Mrs. Besant wishes to deserve this epitaph :
“ She tried to follow Truth.” She has deserved
it. That the element of truth in Theosophy is
overlaid with much fantastic error matters
little. It is all truth to her, and the grandeur
of her nature glorifies her trivial creed. In
effect, the intellectual belief of a good woman
is of comparatively slight importance. We do
not seek to describe the colour of a flower
before enjoying its beauty or its fragrance.
Heroic souls like Annie Besant, with all their
frailties, are the salt of humanity. They are
linked indissolubly with every generous deed,
with every noble aspiration. They give
courage in the present, and hope for the
future. And here, on this bank and shoal of
time of which alone there is any certitude, we
are proud to yield them honor, whether our
destiny be divinity or dust.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Party Government in Maoriland
There are already certain signs and omens
which portend that Government by Contradic-
tion may yet cause the downfall of the Liberal
Government in Maoriland. The Ministry came
into power with an enormous majority—a
majority so large that, so far as the Lower
House was concerned, it could pass almost any
measures it chose. Premier Ballance, fortu-
nately, was not intoxicated by so much autho-
rity, and never in any way abused it. But
Seddon, who has inherited his predecessor’s
majority, has a tendency to drive his followers
much too hard, and there are already symptoms
of revolt. Minister M‘Kenzie’s insane Libel
Bill, which provides that every newspaper-writer
shall append his full name and address to all
his articles —a measure which is commonly
believed to have its origin in M'Kenzie’s desire
to lay the large red hand of identification on
his own personal critics —is the worst of various
small and irritating follies which the Seddon
Cabinet has lately committed. The House would
gladly throw it out, but under the clumsy system
of Party Government, it is impossible to do it
without a serious risk of throwing out the Liberal
Ministry altogether. Therefore, the choice
lies between swallowing the Seddon policy
whole or else putting the Conservative party in
power, and the Maoriland Conservative party
is probably the most widely-hated political
combination in Australasia. There is practi-
cally no alternative between these two ex-
tremes ; the party system is based on the
principle that the Legislature must exercise no
power of discrimination, but must accept the
policy of the Government in its entirety, or
else put its opponents in power and accept
their policy in its entirety instead. Therefore,
Maoriland will put up with the small follies of
the Seddon Cabinet as best it can until, at last,
in a sudden burst of aggravation, it will pro-
bably go back on its Liberalism for a time, and
help a Conservative reaction. The House
would gladly point out to Seddon that his
manners are not all that can be desired, and
that his colleague, M'Kenzie, is a clumsy per-
son of the bull-in-a-china-shop description, and
it would very much like to make some serious
alterations in Ward’s latest budget; but it can’t
do these things without endangering all that has
been gained since Ballance first came into
power, so it endures Seddon and M‘Kenzie as
best it can. If it could give them notice to quit
without thereby bursting up its entire Demo-
cratic policy it would probably do so, but the
system of Government by Contradiction forbids.
The fact that the greatest progressive move-
ment ever initiated in Australasia should come,
by a pure accident, to depend on the coarse and
aggressive ex-publican’s retention of his billet is
a strange anomaly, but the constitution is full
of such anomalies. No one ever knows in
politics what kind of character the best cause
may ultimately depend on.
The Land Resumption Dodge.
The little strip of land in Lavender Bay
(Sydney) resumed for the construction of the
Milson’s Point railway has cost the State
.£4:0,000. The area is just over six acres, so the
price is .£6500 per acre, or .£3OO per foot front-
age. Richardson and Wrench’s valuation,
about two years ago, was .£27,000, and the
decline in prices since then has probably brought
the market value down below <£20,000. Evi-
dently the poor old Treasury has been badly
got at, as it always is. Under the present
system, too, there is absolutely no means of
preventing the Treasury being got at. Taking
it all round, the State, when it buys land for
public purposes, pays twice or three times as
much for it as does anybody else. This is one
of the places where a land-tax, run on the
Maoriland system, would come in. Let every-
body be allowed to value his own land, the
State reserving—and freely exercising in cases
of attempted grab—the right to take the land
at the owner’s valuation and sell or lease it for
* u ‘* kanofi*-. nf the revenue. Then, when Govern-
ment wants to resume a patch of ground for any
purpose the owner will get the price which
he put upon it for taxation purposes, and no
more. With such a law as this, and an efficient
Betterment Act, New South Wales, Victoria,
and S.A. could even now begin building rail-
ways out of revenue, and the cost on which
interest would require to be paid would be so
small compared with that under the old system
that the lines could be made profitable in
many places where they would otherwise be
absolutely bankrupt. Of the many millions
which have already been spent in railroad con-
struction throughout Australia probably half
might have been saved if an equitable policy
had been adopted from the beginning, ami then
the railways would have been a splendid asset
instead of being, as they practically are, a
liability. But more than 90 per cent, of the
railway system is still to be built, so there is
time to save many more millions and tens of
millions by an immediate adoption of entirely
new principles.
The Rising of Young Victoria.
A few weeks ago The Bulletin, believing
Victoria to be still unready for an outbreak of
popular feeling, predicted that a dissolution of
its Pattersonian Parliament would result in a
reinstatement of nearly all the old members,
whilst the country went on “ sneering feebly as
before.” The prediction has been falsified con-
siderably. and falsified even more in spirit than
in fact. With the announcement of a general
election there came a sudden change in the Vic-
torian public demeanor : an unexpected anima-
tion, a healthy desire to improve the character
of the national Assembly as far as circumstances
would permit, and a noticeable eagerness on
the part of younger voters to nip Patterson’s
freetrade design in the bud. The result was a
return of 31 new members, including an addi-
tion of five to the previous Labor party and the
defeat of three very old-established calamities
in Tom Bent, Bryan O’Loghlen and L. L.
Smith. Of Bent and his political allies The
Bulletin once said that Victoria was waiting
for them to be dead. Well, Bent is now utterly
defunct, nor are the other two gentlemen likely
to achieve a resurrection. Young Victoria,
whose power increases every year, has recorded
an opinion of Bent and Co. for the first time,
and in that opinion there is hope for the future.
Meanwhile, the overthrow of Patterson is a
subject for rejoicing, not so much because any
new Government must necessarily be less
disastrous than the Government that was
smashed to atoms last Thursday, as on account
of the financial policy which was then placed at
the coming Premier’s disposal. An analysis of
candidates’“ views” and promises reveals the
fact that Victoria voted in the first place for
land taxation. George Turner professed to
be waiting for the country’s instructions as to
getting rid of the deficit that Patterson was
afraid to touch. The country has instructed
lawyer Turner. Thirty-five, or more, of the
elected candidates are definitely pledged
to advocate a tax upon unimproved land
values, and another nineteen members have
undertaken to support a bill to that effect.
Against these 50 to 60 adherents of the
land-tax cause there are not 15 sworn
opponents of the principle. The balance of the
Legislature promised nothing in particular
except to shift Patterson. He will be shifted
as per general desire. The Castlemaine bully
stood between capital and the direct tax-
gatherer, and possibly George Turner would
have taken up a similar position if the elections
had turned upon the rival merits of Ministerial
and Opposition policies. But the result of the
poll had very little to do with Turner, other
than as the representative chucker - out of
Patterson, and it had still less to do with
Turner’s nondescript policy. Sixty-six Op-
positionists were returned for the reason, in
almost all cases, that they spoke up for Protec-
tion, and favoured any scheme of direct taxation
which gets money from w here money should come.
Income-tax, property-tax, absentee-tax, wealth-
tax, “ surplus ’’-tax, “direct wealth ’’-tax, and
a progressive land-tax were each and all of
them supported by victorious candidates—a few
candidates being in favour of every kind of
direct tax that their constituents could put a
name to. But the only tax which secured a
strong working majority in the new Parliament
was the tax on unimproved land values. If
Premier Turner chooses to bill that desirable
impost at once, whilst the eye of the country is
fixed upon the matter, it must pass. If he
dodges his responsibility for the sake of banks,
squatters, mortgagors, building societies, &c.,
the wrath of the people will overtake him pre-
sently. Young Victoria has begun to take an
interest in politics, and the doctrine that sena-
tors ought to run straight is being preached by
the rising generation to its boom-tainted parent.
Melbourne’s four daily newspapers were fully
agreed upon one election matter. Each of
them advocated, frankly or by innuendo, the
dismissal of Tom Bent from Parliament. Each
had its mean, contemptible reasons for not
attacking, even if it didn’t champion, many
another candidate who deserved an all-round
“ doing,” but Bent, the Speaker, whose defeat
by a rival Tory could make very little differ-
ence in a “ party ” sense, was the one man they
all objected to on “ public grounds.” Their
refreshing unanimity in publishing three suc-
cessive Bent scandals —the Mercantile Finance
bills story, the parsonic proclamation of Bent’s
“ unfitness to represent any constituency,” and
the damaging ex-parte narrative of how Bent
transferred .£40,000 liability to a day-laborer at
the “alleged” price of 10s.—seemed to justify
a pretty general suspicion that even Patterson
and Co. were quite prepared to see the back of
burly old Tom. The little Standard, which for
all election purposes represented Patterson
body and soul, gloated over each new revela-
tion instead of sup-pressing it, so that Bent,
seeing his name large-typed in his former pal’s
own particular rag, probably knocked his head
against a wall and would have cried, “ Et tu,
Brute ! ” if he had understood the meaning of
that ancient quotation.
— «,
The Queensland Peace Preservation Bill,
although it has passed both Houses, is in a
rather curious situation. While the discussion
was in progress the Speaker suspended for a
week seven Labor members, and during the
period of their suspension the measure was
rushed through the Assembly. Now, it is
asserted, on high legal authority, that the sus-
pensions are altogether unlawful, having been
effected by the Chair on its own sole authority
and not by vote of the House. The Labor
members propose to try the point, and if they
win, and it is decided that their ejection was
unlawful, they will next raise the question
whether the acts of the House done in their
absence are, or are not, null and void. The
common-sense view of the question is that
they are, for otherwise a biassed Speaker could
get anything passed by simply suspending all
opponents ; and, in that case, the Peace Pre-
servation Bill is of no account whatever. In
the meantime the Opposition has petitioned the
Governor to withhold his assent to the Bill
until it is decided whether there is, in a legal
sense, any bill at all to be assented to. A truly
beautiful complication.
There are occasional signs, especially in the
correspondence columns of the daily papers, of
an agitation for a dividend-tax in New South
Wales. A dividend-tax is an impost which
would take so much per cent, from the profits
of banking, trading, mining, insurance, land,
and other companies, while it wouldn’t even
touch the private capitalist who trades, or
mines, or land-swindles on his own private
account and annexes his profits without the
empty formality of declaring a dividend.
The dividend-tax would get at that joint-
stock pawnshop, the Sydney Mont de Piet6,
which lends money at 40 per cent, or
under, while it would pass by pawnbroker
Mosenstein, who charges 100 per shent and
works on his own private account. It would
get at the struggling building society while it
would miss Sydney Burdekin, and it would
levy on the Australian Star and Daily Tele-
graph (and Bulletin) and leave the Herald
and Evening News untouched. It is not a
dividend-tax that the country wants, but an
income, land and absentee tax. And though it
wants the two former badly enough, it wants
the absentee-tax about 150 times worse.
J. M. Cross, the democratic member for Cler-
mont, has a Dill before the Queensland Assembly
providing that any person lending money on
real or personal property shall have no security
outside that property itself. At present —
though very few people are aware of the fact —■
the person who lends on mortgage has not only
the right to sell the property specifically
mortgaged in order to recover his money, but
if it does not realise the required amount he
can pursue his debtor at law for the balance,
and sell him up to the uttermost farthing if
necessary. Therefore, the person who borrows
money on the security of his house or land un-
consciously mortgages not only them but every-
thing else which lie possesses, including his bed
and his wardrobe and all his other belongings.
Further, when the original borrower sells the
property he does not get rid of the original
liability. He may sell his farm subject to the
mortgage, the mortgagee consenting to the
transfer, and the buyer may sell it again, and it
may pass through 20 different hands before any
trouble arises. But if the last holder fails to
pay, and the property does not realise the
amount of the loan, the original borrower of,
perhaps, a dozen years before can still
be sold up for the balance. The per-
son who signs the original promise to pay is
responsible to the bitter end. This cruel in-
justice has hitherto attracted little attention,
because, owing to the steady rise in property
values, the mortgagee has generally got his
pound of flesh without any difficulty. But the
recent fall in prices has made his security, in
hundreds of cases, worth less than the amount
of the advance, and as a consequence the
original borrower, in the comparatively few'
instances where he is still solvent, is having a
bad time of it. An immediate reform in the
law relating to mortgages is much wanted.
Re Haynes’s proposal to abolish capital
punishment and legal torture in New South
Wales that wild, whirling, ungrammatical
person, Benbow Q. Scalplock, of Bathurst
National Advocate, speaks :
Sympathy with crime is one of the most awful evi-
dences of a nation’s retrogression ; and this sympathy
is so much shown in Australia that it sometimes causes
thoughtful men to tremble for the race’s future. In no
other part of the world is murder so prevalent, serious
crime so common, property and liberty so little respected.
With only 4,000,000 of a population hardly a single week
passes by without our having to read of a double or triple
murder, of a ghastly doing to death by father, mother,
or sweetheart, of a brutal outrage on a helpless woman,
or of a shocking offence against the purity and innocence
of a little child. Crime is rampant. . . . The rope
and lash are the necessary means for avenging the victims
of certain brutal crimes, which mere incarceration in no
way provides against. And instead of the offences being
lessened in number which come under the extreme
remedies of law, they should be increased, and increased
again until criminality is finally choked out of existence
by the great fear of those who are criminally inclined.
The Bulletin hereby draws the attention of
the hysterical Scalplock to the fact that Aus-
tralia is not the most criminal part of the globe;
it comes on the list of civilised states some
distance behind France, Prussia, Saxony, most
of the other German states, Italy, Sweden, and
an enormous distance behind the United States.
Also, it has fewer murders in proportion to
population than France, Austria, Hungary,
Italy, Spain, the U.S., and about two dozen
other countries. Further, crime is diminishing,
rather than increasing, in proportion to popu-
lation ; and, in any case, the man who talks
about “ choking crime out of existence ” is an
ignoramus. Even in the days when 2000
persons were executed yearly in England crime
wasn’t “ choked.” Australia has little to boast
of in the matter of criminality, but it can’t be
improved by the frantic assertions of the
National Advocate, or the bloodstained
frenzy of Brother Scalplock, as he wades
through gore and bathos and figurative muddle-
ment, and winds up with a horrid cry for more
blood and lashes.
Judge Williams, enlarging upon the prepos-
terous cost of Vic. railway lines under
Speight’s management, seems to have over-
looked one vital point in the evidence.
Speight, having received a corrupt Legisla-
ture’s order to buy the land necessary for a
new line, was quite at the mercy of the
vendors. In 29 of the “ worst cases,” as his
counsel piit it, Speight went to arbitration,
and in 27 out of the 29 cases the arbitrators
decided that the Railway Department had not
offered enough to the landowners. “In those
cases the Commissioners had to pay costs on
both sides amounting to .£2473. Where the
Commissioners succeeded they got <£39 costs
from the two land-owners concerned. After
this experience of arbitration in land-purchase,
the Commissioners gave it up.” The arbi-
trators, on an average, awarded about 28 per
cent, more purchase-money than the railway
valuators offered, and this in instances where
they had been forced into offering what they
considered an extravagant price.
A slab of Justice Williams’s libel-law:—
“Nearly every point in the case (Speight v.
Syme) could be used to illustrate the defences
of justification and fair comment, and on the
plea of fair comment the jury had to look at
the facts which had been proved by defendant
to have existed at the time the comments or
criticisms were made, and not to Mr. Speight’s
or his witnesses’ explanations. On the plea of
justification, which had to do with the truth of
the charges, they must, of course, regard the
explanations and evidence of Mr. Speight, but
for the purposes of fair comment they had, if
possible, to put themselves in the position of
never having heard a word from Mr. Speight
or any of his witnesses.” The Williams theory
of fair comment is even more satisfactory to
certain editors than the Hodges theory, ex-
pounded at the first Speight v. Syme trial. A
public man who is frequently seen entering or
leaving a brothel may be called a “notorious
fornicator”—according to Judge Williams.
He brings an action for libel against the
newspaper that refers to his scandalous
conduct, and he proves that he went to the
bagnio in a purely missionary capacity. His sole
business there was to convert the residents.
He satisfies the jury that he acted as a Sama-
ritan. But the jury must look at the
facts which existed before the politician’s
religious fervor became known. They must
say that it was fair to draw the worst conclusions
from his visits to the house of evil reputation,
and find for the defendant journal. In criminal
law a man is supposed to be innocent until his
guilt is proved. In fair comment libel , law a
man starts by being very guilty, and remains
equal to guilty after his innocence has been
established. A peculiarity of “ fair comment”
is its restriction to “great” newspapers only.
A little newspaper, The Bulletin imagines,
has no claim to this line of defence. The mis-
statements of a cheerful publication, as dis-
tinguished from the lies of a penny news-sheet,
are scurrilous, blackguardly, infamous, and
utterly revolting. Fair comment is a privilege
of the organs that play the dullest tunes.
Dear Bulletin, —The worst feature about the
Paraguayan exodus is the way in which Lane’s
followers and professed friends have rounded
on him. Several of those who fawned on
him when he was popular are noM de-
nouncing him. I see that one of the disaffected
Parargohauts, a noted jawster, on being aiked
his opinion of the English labor movement,
gave it as his mature belief, after a whole
week’s observation, that they were dreadfully
split up from gangrened jealousy. This would
not be an invidious statement if it came from
anyone else but the man who rounded on his
political father, Lane. After all, Lane,
with his ideals destroyed, his hopes blasted,
and all his airy castles tumbled to the
ground, is worth a whole shipload of the
dogs who licked from his plate in his prosperous
days, but who yelp at his heels now that he is
discredited. Lane’s head was large enough to
contain schemes for the beatification of all
humanity. He gave up assured literary success,
and certain worldly reward, for his ideal. He
has failed, because the day for crusaders has
gone by, the age of chivalry is dead, and enter-
prises of that kind now attract only the cranks,
the permanently dissatisfied, and the mentally
unbalanced. A latter day Don Quixote, Lane
never allowed for the windmills (nor the wind-
bags), and even his Sancho Panzas were
humbugs always prepared to steal his
kingdom and his brains. Future generations
will regard Lane as an heroic figure in an
unheroic age, and history will forgive him for
what he failed to do, and remember vW. be
tried.—Yours, J. D. Fitzgerale
The suggestion to pay New South Wales M.’sP.
so much per attendance, a maximum total
being- fixed, is not new. It was tried in
Queensland, in the days of the Griffith 10-
millions-loan regime. The pay which that
master of snuffle and shuffle dictated was—
“two guineas per sitting; no member to be
allowed more than .£2OO in one year.” The
Clerk of Assembly was required, within a
certain time limit—if the writer remembers
rightly, half-an-hour—to log the names of all
members who had showed their noses by then.
Those who came in afterwards didn’t count,
except —so rumour said —by special request.
The system, as may be at once guessed, led to
queer antics. It was great fun to see
Mugwump, M.P., put in an easy appearance,
stay quietly until the “attendance log ” was
duly entered up, and then disappear for the
evening. The only good the system effected
was to limit sessions practically to 95 nights.
The idea of Bavistek, M.P. (N.S.W.), to pay
per attendance at divisions only, would never
have suited Mugwump, of ,Banan aland. The
whip would have had to scoftr the theatres, the
pubs., the oyster-saloons, the cottages omies,
and the police-cells to round him up in any
respectable number. For of such is the “piece-
work ” legislator.

passion-fruit? It is the commonest of Australian

you could not buy it in England for gold. It only
wants a start to be immensely popular in Europe.

The policy-holders of the Colonial Mutual Life
Assurance Society— next to the A.M.P. the hipest con-
cern of its kind in Australia, and after the A..M.P. and
the Maoriland Govt. Insurance Department, the biggest in
the southern hemisphere—would do well to make some
inquiry as to how their funds are employed. Judging
solely by the figures in the balance-sheet, the inference is
that the Colonial Mutual must have made some very
unlucky investments. For a year or two back its
funds have yielded the lowest rate but one among
the six hig Australian offices, the Mutual of Austra-
lasia having the very lowest. This in itself is
rather a good feature, for high interest gener-
ally goes with very bad security. The Colonial Mutual,
however, seems to have got low interest and a consider-
able amount of inferior security as well. Its funds
amount to £1,662,301, and of this £530,336 is in “ Free-
hold and Leasehold Property.” Bricks and mortar are
not the best kind of property, and the Colonial Mutual
apparently has more of them than any other office in
Australasia. It has a larger percentage of its money in
furniture and sundries than any other office except the
A.M.P. Also, in the matter of uncollected interest and
rents it breaks the record. The following table is
interesting:—
Interest and Ditto, Out-
Rents. standing and
1893. Accrued.
A.M.P. .. .. „ £668,793 £54,725
Mutual of Victoria .. 44,762 11,925
Mutual of Australasia 52,517 14.046
National Mutual .. 71,047 19,059
Widows' Fund ... 55 798 11,081
M.L. Government ... 108,097 27,127
Colonial Mutual .. 81,026 55,743
The Colonial Mutual has thus considerably
more than twice as large a percentage of interest and
rente outstanding as any other of the seven offices above
quoted. What the policy-holders ought to know is: (1)
How much, if any, of this is absolutely irrecoverable ; (2)
In these cases, if there are any, where the interest is not
recoverable, what is the prospect of recovering the prin-
cipal? The evil is apparently getting worse, for whereas
this item, at the end of '92, stood at £39,324, at the end
of ’93 it had increased to £55,743. Last year’s financial
disasters struck all the offices more or less by reducing
the rate of interest derived from investments, and also, to
some extent, by increasing the difficulty of collecting
such interest as there was, but in the latter respect the
Colonial Mutual seems to have had far worse luck than the
average. If the directors have any satisfactory explana-
tion to offer it should be made at once. So far as can be
Judged from the outward semblance of things, either the
Colonial Mutual has the worst batch of investments among
the seven big offices mentioned—or else some of the other
six have artistically improved their balance-sheets by
adding the overdue interest to the principal under the
head of “mortgages.” Until there is a systematic State
audit such a thing is quite possible.
The Maoriland Government Department—the
only Slate-audited office in the crowd—is also the only
one which keeps the “ accrued ” and the “outstanding ”
interest separate in its balance-sheet. The former repre-
sents interest earned but not yet payable; the la'ter is
interest overdue but not paid, and it is this latter item
which has a tendency to develone into bad debts. The
Maoriland Govt, office has only £2311 actually overdue ;
the other £24,815 is only “accrued." Why don’t the
Australian insurance societies adopt a similar system?
• • • ♦
In reference to the remarks made in last week’s
Bulletin as to the relative expenses of various Australian
life-insurance societies, there is one aspect of the case
which was, perhaps, not made sufficiently clear. A large
part of an insurance office’s expenses consists of commis-
sions and medical charges connected with new policies.
Business is expensive to acquire, but costs, or should cost,
comparatively little to retain when once secured. There-
fore the expenses of a new office, where a very large pro-
portion of the business is in this initiatory stage, are
generally greater than those of an old one, and an institu-
tion which does a large new business usually shows a
larger expense account than one which is less progressive.
Last year the City Mutual had by far the largest expense
account among Australasian insurance offices, but in pro-
portion to its size its new business was three times as
large as that of the Mutual of Victoria, nnd considerably
larger than that of the A.M.P. or Colonial Mutual, or
even the National Mutual, which comes second nn the
list in this respect. Also, the City Mutual reports the
largest percentage of interest on its invested funds—
about £6 Bs. per cent, against the other offices’ average of
about £5 17s.

The new Proprietary assistant-manager, duly
impressed with the contrast between the methods of work
in Spanish mines and those of Australia, is reported to be
already on the track of several startling reforms. One
idea is said to be the substitution of day-work for the
present contract system in working the open outs;
whereat there is mingled quaking and gnashing of teeth
on the part of several large contractors, who have found
the open cuts a happy hunting-ground.
* * * ♦
The syndicate, consisting principally of J. S.
Keid, which twelve months ago started work on several
Euriowie tin properties with extensive dressing-plants
erected during the boom era, has lost heavily on the
venture, and has shaken the dust of Euriowie off its feet
and turned Westward. Which is a pity, as the syndicate,
besides making an honest effort to resurrect the prosperity
of the field through its own properties, gave encourage-
ment to other mine-owners and tributors by dressing ore.
Few, if any, of the latter can now continue at work, and
the syndicate’s failure is the practical collapse of the
Euriowie field.

* • * *
Gold or whisky is at the bottom of most wars,
and the Corean war is no exception. Corea is a strip of
country, with nothing but hills, of about the same size as
England and Scotland combined, and on the mountain
range across its middle are the famous Phyong Kang gold
washings. Previous to the Japanese Treaty in ’76 these
produced hut small quantities of gold-dnst and speck
nuggets, all of which went to the king. The Japs, how-
ever, got these worked better. Concessions were granted,
and in ’B2 £70,000 worth of gold-dust was exported, and
this has now risen to about £250,000 annually. This
trade is almost entirely in the hands of the Japanese, and
there is no doubt that the possession of these workings is a
larger factor in the cause of the war than is generally
realised. The sum paid to the King of Corea now is one
onnoe of gold per month tor each gang of 30 men engaged
in the work.
* * * •
The most nerveful thing in the way of company-
promoting which The Bulletin remembers is the “Cool-
gardie Golden Nugget Syndicate.” This enterprise is being
got up by the “ Real Estate Investment and Agency,”
of 250 Pitt-street, Sydney. The “ Investment and
Agency” doesn't supply any capital, or accept any
responsibility, but it takes one-twelfth of all profits. The
object of the syndicate is to send Mr. A. H. W. Hill to
Coolgardie to look for a reef, and Mr. Hill takes a fourth
of the profits, so that he and the “Investment and
Agency ” get away with one-third between them. Who
Mr. Hill is, or what are his special qualifications in the
way of finding reefs, is not explained. The “Investment
and Agency," which doesn’t supply one cent, of the
coin, or take any risks whatever, and which only owns
one-twelfth of the shares, is to nominate three out
of the six directors, the people who find all
the cash nominating the other three f and it
is provided by article 111. of the agreement that the
directors shall do pretty much as they darn well please.
The “Investment and Agency” gets all the cash mean-
while, and spends it, seemingly, anyhow it likes, there
being no expressed provision for any audit, or any publi-
cation of accounts. Also, the “Investment and Agency ”
runs all the office business, and keeps the books, and
generally looks after things.
According to the prospectus: “In the S.M.
Herald of Uth August, Mr. Alex. Forrest (a member of
the W.A. Government) speaking at Perth, said ‘Mr.
Dunn was of opinion that the Wealth of Nations mine
was worth at least £1,000,000 to-day.’ If Mr. Hill finds
another Wealth of Nations mine for this syndicate the
return to each holder of a £1 share would be about
£2000.” To which it might as reasonably be added that
if Mr. Hill finds 160 such mines for the syndicate, each £1
shareholder will get t'320,000. But then he won’t. Such
mines are not plentiful, to begin with, and the man who
finds such a mine generally finds it for himself, not for a
syndicate. In the matter of assurance the Golden Nugget
Syndicate seems all right, but as a gold-mining enterprise
it looks a gruesome frost.
• * • »
Inspector Lee is a little too definite in describ-
ing reefs at Hargrave, N.S.W., as “real saddle-reefs
similar to those on Bendigo.” In many places in N.S.W.
small reefs which have the appearance of “saddles” can
be found ; but, after a little work, they either give way
in the legs or disappear altogether; none are fit to be
compared with Bendigo formations either in size or
permanency.

A certain Southern master-brickmaker, who
owns, in close proximity to his various brick-yards, many
scores of houses, engages as workmen only those who
agree to become his tenants and pay him from 12s. to 15s.
for the privilege of living in houses which, at present
rent-rates, are worth not more than from Ss. to 10s. per
week. Compared with the average landlord of to-day,
who, when he calls round for his rent, generally finds the
tenant and the hack-gate missing, this individual sits on
velvet. The tenant who knocks about his property
simply has the amount of the alleged damage— at the
landlord’s own bloated valuation—deducted from his
week's wage and promptly gets “sacked,” for in these
workless days there are plenty of workmen only too ready
to fill the vacancy, and have their lives gradually
squeezed out of them. Moreover, no had debts are con-
tracted by this mode of business, the rent being deducted
every pay-day from the workmen’s wages. This trucker
consoles himself, too, that when a Truck Bill is passed it
will not interfere with his monopoly, because, as he
explains, he does not actually deduct the rent when pay-
ing the men, but simply counts out the full amount of
wages due and at the same time holds out his paw for the
rent, for which he gives a Receipt. No Truck Bill will be
complete unless it includes this sort of person
Woman fco the front again. Misa I za
Coghlan, M.B. (Sydney), and Miss Grace Clara
Stone, M.B. (Melbourne), have been appointed
medical referees to the A.M.P. Society.
The assiduous dropping of the Sir, by
Graham Berry, in his election, ought certainly to
be brought under the notice of the Garter King
at Arms, or somebody. The first batch of posters
went out with “ Sir Graham Berry,” but a hench-
man told him it would be sudden death.
That amusing toy, the Comte de Paris, left
behind him a written opinion that God has not yet
abandoned France. The country, wrote the fatu-
ous Bourbon person, has lost a lot of its religious
sentiment, its former faith in royal lines has gone
seriously astray, but God in His mercy continues
to protect the people. The idea is that He wil]
never desert France as long as there is one genuine
Bourbon pretender left to pray for a restoration of
the monarchy.
Sydney D.T. tries to embastion N.S.W.
Premier Reid by quoting half-a-col. from London
Daily Chronicle including this :
Mr. Reid has realised that the greatest political need o
Australia is a good system of local Government.
The Chronicle evidently believes that N.S.W.
means all Australia, and that all Australia is
destitute of local Government! Such are the
opinions which Australians are asked to respect
by their own daily press !

Dear Bulletin,—! am a Protectionist
second, and a Federalist first. I therefore favour
the assimilation of Vic. tariffs with S. A., Queens-
land, and M.L. —but only on condition that
assimilation in any particular is immediately
followed by Freetrade, in the articles concerned,
between the provinces having identical tariffs. In
two previous letters I have enlarged on the idea
of interprovincial reciprocity, and I write this
postscript in view of the Vic. elections.— Fluffy.

Bachelor Zox, the East Melb. candidate,
was deploring at one of his meetings the scarcity
of brawny arms in Victoria. “ What we need is
a larger population to develope our magnificent
country,” &0., &0., shouted dear old Zox. “Then,
why don’t you get married ? ” enquired an
and the paunchy patriot had to laugh at himself
with the rest of the meeting. It was another Jew,
probably, who fired off this happy little joke.
And maybe Zox himself had planned the incident
with his constituent. An artful old bird, Ephraim
Zox.
The plural voter was mighty busy in Mel-
bourne and outlying districts last Thursday. He
must have plucked two or three Tories from the
burning, besides reducing the large natural
majorities of a dozen Liberal poll-toppers. How-
ever, he served one good purpose, did the plural-
voter. When he found himself on the roll for
Brighton—a p.v. always “ finds himself on the
roll ” and doesn’t know who put him there—he
generally went down to that breezy suburb and
ran a blue pencil mark through the name of Bent.
The plural-voter believed in having a different
sort of Speaker, you know. Otherwise he loved
Bent dearly.
Brisbane Figaro sometimes moves and
wriggles as if alive; sometimes, like other snakes,
it is curled up and comatose. A while ago Figaro
was apparently dead for good, but as the gang in
office had sores which needed licking, our loath-
some contemporary arose once more and went to
work. Figaro aforesaid now says that The Bul-
letin “is controlled, if not owned, by the Bank
of New Zealand.” As that foundationless lie
may go round (Figaro being bought regularly at
printers’ job-rates and mailed through Queens-
land by people with axes to grind), it may be as
well here to say that neither the B.N.Z. nor any
other bank, firm, institution, or person, apart
from the officially-registered proprietors, has any
financial claim on, share in, or control over The
Bulletin.
The libel-action brought against Brisbane
Telegraph by Sub-Inspector Carr (for publishing
a telegram stating that the Sub-Inspector had
been arrested for the Coombemartin shooting
business), is down for trial at Rockhampton.
The Telegraph people unsuccessfully sought to
change the venue to Brisbane. It is said that a
big Government official attempted to mediate, and
his efforts so far succeeded that the terms of a
paragraph of explanation were agreed upon by
both parties. Everybody thought the matter
was settled, when Carr’s solicitor demanded his
client’s costs. Telegraph managing-director
Cowlishaw said he would see Carr emphatically
blanked first, and the negotiations ceased.
Both the Argus and the Age lied in the
most preposterous manner all through the Vic-
torian elections. Take a lovely instance. Here
are two accounts of the same meeting, one held
by Mr. Murray Smith at Hawthorn. Argus
account: —
Mr. Murray Smith rose to receive such a reception as is
seldom accorded to a public man. The immense audience
rose to its feet, every hat was waved, and a tremendous
cheer arose, completely drowning out the hoots in the
back benches.
Now read the Age version of the same meeting :
The candidate went on to address the meeting, but the
hostility was too pronounced, and there was a continued
uproar which drowned Mr. Smith’s utterances. . . .
Mr. Smith continued his efforts to speak, but the meeting
would have none of him and got out of the chairman’s
control, with the result that he closed his remarks in a
few minutes.
As a matter of fact, both accounts were untrue.

Mrs. Annie Besant made her first appear-
ance in Sydney at the Opera House on Tuesday
0 [ last week, and established herself as a success
within ISmin. from the beginning of her lecture.
The chief drawback connected with the proceed-
ings was the Opera House itself, which is old and
dingy and cow-like in its aspect, and wears the
dusty, neglected, moth-eaten expression of an
orphan in despair. Partly, no doubt, by reason
of the gloomy and battered semblance of the old
house, the audience was not a cheerful-looking
one . The same crowd might have looked cheerful
in the bright Centenary Hall, but against the
faded pea-greenish background it looked a most
anaesthetic gathering. The Bulletin is prepared
to swear that every plain woman of uncertain age
in all Sydney —every woman who is either gaunt,
yellow, bony, wrinkled, shrivelled, tired, depressed,
sour or expressionless, every woman who dresses
in rusty black and wears large shapeless boots,
likewise every woman who has a hungry soul
looking out through two leaden eyes—was there
on the occasion. So was every man who has a
shaved upper lip, and a monkey beard, and a long
inky tail-coat, and a collar which isn’t so white as it
might be, and a shirt-front which has got crumpled
through the owner looking after “higher things.”
There was a row of elderly, daraaged-looking
women on the platform, and Judge Windeyer as
chairman. Judge Windeyer as an orator is just
one remove from wretched, and on his high
pedestal his legs looked disgracefully short, and
his body rotund, and the whole of him hopelessly
unpoetio. There was not one bright feature in
the whole dimly-lighted assemblage save Mrs,
Besant herself, with her pleasant, intellectual
face, and her silvery hair, and her white gown,
and tlie mystic ring which she inherited from the
boss visionary of her creed, Madame Blavatsky,
displayed on her strong white hand.
* * *
The impression she made, both at this and
subsequent lectures, was altogether favorable. No
other such eloquent woman, and rarely a more
eloquent man, has been heard in Australia before.
.She has no gestures, no attitudes, and—save a
tendency to exhibit the deceased Blavatsky’s ring
—no affectations. Her voice is clear, and her
diction faultless. Also, she is, in The Bulletin’s,
opinion, the most absolutely sincere missionary
who ever came to this country. Some of her
opinions would not look well on the top of a copy-
book, but all of them would look well anywhere
else. She denounces the sending of “ Christian ”
missionaries to the Moslem and the Buddhist
and the men of those other sects which are
commonly lumped together as “heathens.” The
only way, she says, to benefit the “heathen” is
to teach him to live up to the creed he has ; not to
compress him violently into a misfit faith which is
utterly unsuited to his temperament. She objects
to the trading instincts of the age, and wants to
make a bonfire of such books as the accursed
Samuel Smiles’ “Self-help,” and the tale of
“Samuel Budgett, the Successful Merchant,” and
the rest of the literature which deifies the small
boy that starts life with 6d., and rises through
patient industry to be a bloated and much-esteemed
grocer, and then dies and has a huge monument
piled with a sickly squash on to his bones. A
deep and pathetic love for humanity pervades all
her utterances, and she will probably make many
converts to Theosophy simply because she pre-
sents it as the religion of human kindliness and
brotherhood. Her comparative failure to explain
the meaning of her creed is probably owing to the
hopeless mistiness of the creed itself.
• • •
Mrs. Besant leaves Theosophy very much
the same large, loose, yearnful semblance of a
stupendous shade which it was before. Whatever
it may be to the person who believes, to tile person
who—like the present writer —wants to believe
and can’t it is merely a great, blubliery, shape-
less idea, which crawls with a beneficent intent
upon its stomach all the days of its life, and never
arrives at any defined place or purpose. It is full
of visions and vast, soulful imaginings, and of
mahatlnas whom no one ever sees, and re-incarna-
tions of which there is no evidence; and it has not
even the advantage, like the Old Testament creed,
of resting on the bald and well-defined assertion
of some legendary seer who is dead now, so that
it is impossible to prove that he was never alive.
But, whatever her creed may be, there is no room
to doubt Mrs. Besant’s sincerity, her eloquence,
her nobility of character, and her sympathy with
suffering in all its forms. And, after hearing her
persuasive deliverances, the present writer, who is
very tired of everything and badly wants to be re-
incarnated, if satisfactory arrangements can be
made to that effect, is quite willing to be a Theo-
sophist if he only knew how to begin. The
trouble is that Theosophy seems to be a globular
quicksand, with no beginning.
