* * *
Maggie Moore also announces the last
night of “ Struck Oil ”at Sydney Royal—in fact,
Sydney is placarded all over with last nights, and
everything seems to be coining to an end. On
Friday next a matinee is to be given to the in-
mates of the local charitable institutions, and the
halt and the blind and the toothless-will all be
admitted free. It should be a truly solemn
occasion when these pathetic wrecks are
gathered together. But probably the saddest
feature about this dead-alive audience will be that
it won’t go out to see another human wreck
between the acts, because there will hardly be the
price of a drink in the whole audience. Consider
the pathos of it—that great assemblage of dry
cripples who never expect to be decently moist
again as long as they live until they are washed
just before burial ! The spectacle will be more
depressing than an execution, and the man who
wants to be present at such a function must have
a fine taste for melancholy.
* * ifc *
“Sowing the Wind,” the new play at
Melb. Princess’s, has one chief idea in common
with “Sweet Lavender.” Sydney Grundy, the
author, seems to have purposely drawn attention
to this fact by locating the first scene at Bar-
chester. From Bar/tohester, we remember,
Pinero’s banker came up to London to warn his
adopted sou against a mesalliance with little
Lavender, the result being the banker’s discovery
that Lavender was his natural daughter by a
sweetheart whom he had naturally betrayed and
deserted. The banker’s replica in “Sowing the
Wind ” is a Barchester man (Titheradge). He
learns with horror of his adopted son’s (Cecil Ward
again) mad infatuation for a lovely vocalist (Mrs,
Brough), of doubtful antecedents. Then Tith.,
the father by adoption, tells C.W. the story of
his own earlier days—how he, too, had wor-
shipped a strange goddess, and was only
saved from disastrous matrimony by the
exertions of his friends, Lord Boncicault and dear
old bachelor Brough, Lord Boucioault told
damaging stories about the goddess, whilst
Brough, an amateur man of the world, took the
truth of them for granted. Thanks to Brough
and his wise saws, Titheradge had shaken himself
free from the girl and achieved a respectable,
gouty old age. This story falls to work the
desired effect. Cecil Ward, a gallant johnnie
loafing on a handsome allowance, remains true to
the doubtful vocalist, and the old gent from Bar-
ohester lives to learn (although the shock nearly
carried him off) that the lady is the result of his
conquered passion aforesaid, born, like Lavender,
unbeknown to her illegitimate father. Titheradge
finds, moreover, that Ids child has been slandered
even as the mother was falsely accused at the
time he, her lover, listened to good advice and
behaved badly. A happy denouement is assisted
by Lord Boucioault, who cynically admits that he
worked upon Brough’s ignorance, and used the
shallow, sham judge of woman character as a tool.
After making winch statement Lord Boucioault
departs, leaving poor old bachelor Brough to weep
for his well-meaning stupidity.
* * *
“ Sowing the Wind ” abounds in fine epi-
grammatic writing, has some strong dramatic
situations, and is altogether a beautiful play.
Subordinate characters, intended to lighten tfie
story, dou’c lighten it much, more’s the pity, but
they are good as far as they go. The serious
interest comes to a head in a great dialogue be-
tween Mrs. Brough and Titheradge at the end of
the third act, which won three recalls on Saturday
night._ Yet none of the principals did full justice
to their parts on this trying occasion, and the per-
formance will doubtless improve rapidly. Mrs.
Brough was too loud and Paula Tanquerayish,
Titheradge’s emotion often lacked warmth, and
both the B.’s might have been a little more for-
cible. Cecil Ward, who is elegantly draped in a
green suit, can add a few details, whilst Miss
Noble, as a giggling virgin, will have to get a con-
sistent tone of voice. Mr. Dorrington plays
acceptably the part of a loutish baronet, and Miss
Romer, as a frivolous old piece of gentility, is
capital. It is a costume play, dated 70 years
back, for the sake of old-fashion effects, Goatcher
has painted a glorious garden-scene for the first
act, and the dresses and grouping of figures
against this bright background suggest a picture
by Marcus Stone. There is only a fortnight left
for “ Sowing the Wind ” to do great business in.
■ifc * * *
The third weekly effort of the Taylor-
Carriugton Co. at Melb, Royal is an improvement
on its dreary predecessors. An innocent convict
and a bloated squatter sustain a long, exciting
contest for the hand of Ella Carrington, and the
evil land-owner, who includes bushranging among
his many vices, gets wiped out. He seems to
prosper for a while, especially when his lawless
gang bear the substantial Miss Carrington away
to their mountain cave, but the other fellow ulti-
mately clears his character with a written con-
fession, and the lady looks as happy as she can,
considering that business is not very brisk.
“Unjustly Sentenced” includes a well-known
Melbourne horse and many other features of Aus-
tralian interest, such as new-churns on the wal-
laby track. A further appeal to national
sentiment will be made on Saturday by “ The
Bush King,” which is said to have been written
here and successfully produced at three Loudon
theatres.
*# * *
Melb. Alexandra re-opened on Saturday
under the management of Inigo Tyrrell, who
is likewise the author and star supporter of “The
Life we Live.” Just now is not the time for
abusing Inigo’s drama, for events more horrible
than this have transpired freely in Melbourne
since Easter. In the light of recent experience
“The Life we Live” appears almost reasonable,
although the hero, a harmless little person, pro-
mises never to get drunk any more. But, as
regards Inigo Tyrrell’s attempt at low comedy,
language cannot meets its liabilities, therefore The
Bulletin prefers to remain silent. A diamond-
drill, a man suspended from a burning
telegraph - wire, and a variety entertain-
ment occur in the course of Inigo’s horror.
The gallery “guyed” this variety festival on
Saturday, but the author-actor-rnanager announced
in Monday’s advts. that he had engaged a com-
petent working staff in place of the “ bunglers ”
who had tried to kill his great work. Hans
Phillips, J. G. Joyce and old Harry Daniels are
satisfactory items in a small oast. Also, Miss
Millie Collier acts with plenty of intelligence and
a pair of nicely-balanced arms.

pany and a programme which is confidently ex-
pected to catch on. Phil. Stuart has the directing
of this new enterprise, and a number of gorgeous
particulars will be disclosed shortly.

It is said that when the Lyric Club
amateurs first engaged Johnny Wallace to put
them through an opera the girls sidled up to that
remorseless veteran, and, having coughed several
times in a coaxing key, told him how nervous he
made them feel. His reputation for strictness had
preceded him, they said, and their sweet timid
glances seemed to suggest hysterics on very short
provocation. Johnny, therefore, used to struggle
with his feelings at rehearsal, until one day the
club saw plainly enough that the stage manager
must either swear or burst. He had stamped his
foot, and thumped his brow, and ground his
teeth without finding relief, and the club
felt for the wretched man. So the chorus sent
five of their most haughty virgins to Johnny
as a deputation. The deputation stated that they
came to express their heartfelt sorrow for the
club’s stupidity, which was generally admitted by
the club to be maddening to any gentleman of
his (Johnny’s) professional experience, and under
the circumstances the deputation had much
pleasure in saying, on behalf of all the ladies, that
if Mr. Wallace would like to say “Damn ” occa-
sionally he was at perfect liberty to do so. Where-
upon Mr. Wallace said “Damn!” occasionally
and felt much better.

months, at £3O a week, to play Hayden Cotiin’s part in

the “assassinator” again.

A unique specimen of stage-struck girlhood cropped up
in a Melbourne law-case the other week. The young
aspirant in this case had written to Daisy Chard, of Frank
Clark s variety company, slating that she was burning to
go upon the hoards, and undertaking not to make the
other ladies jealous. She was neither “ good-looking nor
anything else, so she wouldn't be in anybody’s way,"
said this poor little novice. Having announced her deter-
mination to come to Melbourne and get an engagement
somehow, she implored Daisy Chard to meet her at the
station, where she might be recognised at a glance by her
superabundance of freckles. Her only claim to distinc-
tion was a yellow, browny-spolted complexion. “lam
always known by my freckles,” she plaintively observed.

When Ryley was playing at London Savoy he was noted
for being lace for rehearsals. One day he was much
hehind-haud, and the genial Gilbert, when he arrived,
was heard to say something about the “ inconvenience of
being kept waiting for such an indifferent actor.” Ryley
bottled up his wrath, and a few days after he returned
from Australia he otianced to meet in the street Gilbert,
who said how pleased he was to see him, and how
delighted to hear of his success in Australia, adding that
he was writing a new opera, and that he had a part which
would just suit him. Ryley, equal to the occasion,
replied that he had secured an engagement, adding,
“And, Mr, Gilbert, I had no idea that you would think so
indifferent an actor worthy to take a part in your opera.”
John Lemmone, the Australian flautist, is to go on
tour with Melba.
Ada Crossley has apparently “ fetched ” Melba. After
Mrs. Vanderveer Green had told the Gippsland girl that
six months under Marches! would put her right at the
top of the tree, Miss Crossley waited on Melba and sang
to her. “I was less nervous than usual, because I was in
such splendid voice. . . . When 1 turned to her there
were tears in her eyes, and she said, ‘ Oh, what a glorious
voice ; I’ve heard nothing like it for years ! If you were
ready you should join me on my concert tour through
America at once. Mme. Marches! has a Miss Mandelssohn
with a fine voice, but it is not to be compared with yours.
I’ve never been so touched in my life. The world wants a
voice like that.’ Of course this was very flattering to me,
and 1 felt it was genuine because she was so excited, and
her manner became at once quite warm and affectionate.

Dear Bulletin, — 1 have been to see ‘‘Lady Winder-
mere’s Pan,” and I venture to sneer at horse hair Wilde’s
collection of colloquial bric-a-brac. My objection is that
the works are too .evident; you see the wheels going round
from start to finish, and are painfully reminded of the
dreadful, decayed British humorist who constructs a five-
shilling situation in order to render possible the birth of
a three-halfpenny pun. Oscar, I take it, collected a list
of epigrams, paradoxes and caustic cynicisms, during his
long literary life, with the deliberate malicious intention
of eventually writing a play round them. What 1 hate
worst, however, is to have one of my cherished notions of
swagger society people at ’Ome upset thus ruthlessly.
Oscar’s swells talk in 20 minutes the smart tilings it took
him Heaven knows how long to manufacture or collect.
The play is supposed to be a reflection of life, the ex-
asthete is content to make it a reflection on life. He
hoped to make society people smart—he made them too
smart and defeated his object.—E.D.
When Lohr was at Gisborne (M.L.) with “Charley’s
Aunt,” he hired an intelligent native to translate the
bill into Maori for the benefit of the local aborigines. The
native took various liberties with the bill, and filled it
with baseless assertions about dancing, juggling, ground
and lofty tumbling, circus ponies and the like, and Lohr
circulated the fraudulent document with a childlike faith
in the honesty of his interpreter. The result was that
250 Mao.is tramped indignantly out after the first
act, and solemnly cursed Lohr by the soul of Hineraoa
and other swears and objurgations, while that misdirected
manager mistook the emotion for applause. He has now
lost all faith in Maori interpreters.
West Coast Times (M.L.) soulfully yeagns over Miss
Adair, the Yankee parachutist, and her balloon ascent :
“ The sensationalism of the act in a man draws crowds of
people, but when a young lady of Miss Adair’s birth and
education, delicately nurtured and reared in the midst of
luxury on her parent’s plantation in Texas, trusts her life
to her trapeze bar and parachute, the coldest and most
unsympathetic of people are roused to a pitch of enihu-
siasm such as is never seen at any other entertainment or
performance." If she had b-en delicately nurtured on
her father’s selection at Boggabri there would probably
have been no enthusiasm worth mentioning.

The custom some newspapers have of giving more oi
less elaborate notice to each performance by a third-rat.
concert co. is a manifest injustice to theatrical managers
Musical “critics ” are, in many cases, the mere creature?
of concert syndicates, and their monotonous yarns about
the same two or three singers and instrumentalists, who
are continually repeating themselves in the same musical
numbers, should be paid for as special advts. But,
perhaps, they are so paid for.
The clerical parent of a popular Southern lady ex-
amateur was at first much averse to his daughter
going on the stage, and exacted a promise from her
that she would not appear professionally while she bore
his name—t.e., remained single! To his horror, her
public appearance followed soon afterwards, but she did
not break her word with her rev. father—she had been
married sub rosa a week previously ! The marriage,
which occurred over a year ago, became known quite
recently to the families concerned and the public.
Mr. Arthur Garner’s benefit is said to be “postponed
indefinitely."
Miss Menk-Meyer, the pianist, is a sister of
Dr. Felix M-yer, well-known in Mel \ theatrical circles.
She is on her way to Australia to visit her family, but
does not intend to give any concerts.

Frank Madden was returned unopposed to
Vic. Assembly at the general election under
peculiar circumstances. A Mr. Bolton came for-
ward in the Liberal interest, and, though he
caused no special enthusiasm, the party accepted
him rather than run two Liberals, and thus split the
vote. He held meetings right up to the day of
nomination, but on that eventful date he and his
deposit failed to turn up. No notice had been
given of his intentions, so there was no time to
get another candidate, and Madden had a walk-
over.
The Bulletin presents the portrait of Mr.
Dowell O’Reilly (on page 10) the young member for
Parramatta, who attained to fame only the other
day by carrying a motion in favor of womanhood
suffrage through N.S.W. Assembly by a large
majority. The motion was mutilated somewhat,
because Mr. O’Reilly drafted it to read that “ the
time had now arrived” when women should
have a vote, and another member carried
an amendment leaving out the assertion that
the time had arrived being evidently of
opinion that the time had always been there
and didn’t need arriving. Still the substantial
glory of the event remains with Dowell O’Reilly.
The member for Parramatta is noteworthy as one
of the few typical Australians who managed
to secure a seat at last N.S.W. elections amid the
frantic inrush of men from Brummagem and other
foreign parts. He secured his place by defeat-
ing, with ease, “ Hughey ” Taylor, an old-estab-
lished nuisance who came to the poll with a letter
of recommendation from Parkes, and was known to
fame as the man who always moved that the
House do now adjourn for tea. Mr. O’Reilly is
the son of Canon O’Reilly, and a graduate of
Sydney University—probably the first graduate
of that institution who is a democrat by
instinct, and who has shown any literary ability
worth mentioning. Further, he is distinguished’
as one of the only two “O’s” in N.S.W. Assembly _

Almost a majority of Mrs. Besant’s audi-
tors are spectacled.
Annie Besant will leave Australia for
Maoriland on her 47th birthday—lst. Oct. next.
George Turner, unless something goes
wrong with his prospects, will be the first Aus-
tralian native to attain the Vic. Premiership.
A sign by the way—“ Michael O’Hara,
surgeon, Cromwell House, Collins-st.” Shade of
St. Patrick ! Cromwell House !!
“I don’t know anything about politics,”
said a Melb. damsel on election-day, “but I do so
want Chief Justice Madden to get in. He’s so
nice.”
The S.M. Herald’s best thing in the
matter of succinct expression for so far of the year
1894: “Miss Shaw is the literary corrective of
Max O’Rell.”
Stephens, of Woolloongabba, who
acted as chairman for burly Jack Annear
on the memorable night when the Labor-
members were suspended, is the most
experienced local-board chairman in Q.
Legislature. A few years ago, he was
Mayor of South Brisbane and chairman
of four divisional boards at one and the
same time.
Another of the self-advertising
gentry who pose as reflectors of Aus-
tralian public opinion, Mr. J. F. Hogan,
M.P., has started for down under, writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent.
He has gone via Canada, and, they say,
has been commissioned to write a book
to be called “Australia Revisited,”
When it comes out it may possibly prove
to be a puff for the new Canada-Pacific
line to Australia.
Messrs. Ward, Lock, and Bowden
(says S.M. Herald) delayed the pub-
lication of Miss Ethel Turner’s book,
“Seven Little Australians,” just as it
was ready, in order to bring out an
American edition simultaneously. The
work, however, is expected very shortly
now. It is running serially in Sylvia’s
Journal, beginning with the August
number. Miss Lilian Turner, a sister
of the clever young Sydney lady men-
tioned above, has just received a wel-
come acknowledgment of her ability in
the art of story-writing. Messrs. Cassell
and Co. recently offered prizes of .£5O,
.£4O, and <£3o for stories, and the other
day a letter reached Sydney from the
editor of Cassell’s Family Magazine
informing Miss Turner that she had won
the <£so prize with her story called “ The
Lights o’ Sydney.”
Henniker Heaton was to start for Aus-
tralia by the Messageries boat on 3rd Sept. He
still maintains that N.S.W. is the only province
which holds out against ocean penny-postage.
Parkes could, it is said, have been chair-
man of Mrs. Besant’s first meeting, but “social
recognition ” was wanted ; and, as Darley was
offish and Duff sleepy, Windeyer was coaxed into
the breach.
F. Villeneuve Smith, late Chief Justice of
Tasmania, will be 75 on Wednesday, Oct. 3. His
son is the chief delight of plaintiffs in affiliation
and criminal-assault cases heard in the Adelaide
police-court.
Upward is the Sergeant-at-arms in the
Victorian Assembly. One of the new members is
Downward. When Downward misbehaves him-
self and is thrown out by Upward then there will
be great doings.

Hon. John Stewart, M.L.C., who has
served in both N.S.W. Houses of Parliament,
is responsible for one, and only one, piece of legis-
lation. He caused Acts of Parliament to be
left unpunctuated.
Mr. A. S. Low, who died suddenly at
Merrylands, Sydney, the other day, after returning
from Peak Hill (and just after assuring his life for
.£8000), was a pioneer of mining at Gulgong,
where he “ struck it rich.”
Evangelist M‘Neill to an Adelaide
audience : “I tell you that many of the people in
this hall are among the meanest things that
crawl.” It was surprising, too, how reverently
they looked up to M‘Neill.
Tom Glassey, leader of the Q. Labor
party, is frequently away from the House ill.
He admits that physically he is not the man he
used to be, but his antipathy to the Tory party
grows stronger every day.
Chief-Secretary Gordon, of S.A., describes
Clement Wragge as “ a meteorological blackleg.”
As Wragge supplies bad weather and droughts at
lower rates than any of his competitors in the
business the name ought to stick.
Last week’s obituary : That snappy writer,
Ross Cox, of Melbourne, who believed with Sir
Walter Scott, “Literature is a good staff, but a
bad crutch.” So he combined the leaderist with
the school-inspector while he could.
Veteran Dean Macartney (Melb.) might
long ago have been Bishop of Melbourne, had he
liked. The see was offered him more than once
after he had passed his three-score years and ten,
but he declined on account of age each time.
Photo. by Kerry & Co.] [George-st., Sydney.
DOWELL O’REILLY, M.P., the proposer of Woman-suffrage
in N.S.W. Assembly,
Parkes is being daily importuned to take
the absurdly-gaudy portrait of himself out of the
vestibule of a Sydney photographic gallery.
Fully a score of people declare they have met the
replica of the portrait among the attendants in the
hall-passage at Monte Carlo,
Explorer Ernest Giles gave up gold-dig-
ding to become a clerk in Melbourne G.P.O. in
the early fifties. From the post-office he went to
the County Court, and resigned to join a Queens
land exploring expedition. He is now leading a
Westralian prospecting expedition.
The chief beneficiary in a recent huge will
was the_ testator’s widow, who arrived in Mel-
bourne in charge of a jewellery show-case at the
Exhibition. While so engaged she captivated
the law-loving f' msus, and she can now get her
cheque honourt 'I i a quarter of a million. What
shall we do with uur girls?
Jerorae K. Jerome, explaining his piracy of
“ Banjo’s ” “ Ace from Snowy River,” says he did
not steal it from The Bulletin, but borrowed it
from an American paper that gives him the
privilege of lifting its stuff. The receiver is as
bad as the thief. Likewise, you can size-up people
by the company they keep.
Alfred Deakin is making a study of the
revolutions of the world, with the idea of writing
a book. He surrounds himself with big histories,
which he reads in tram-cars and in court. It is
not believed that he has any intention of leading
a revolutionary movement in this country. He is
much too respectable for that.
Annie Besant’s face is wrongly described
as “refined.” It is strong and dignified ; but the
features are coarse—the nose short and thick ; the
mouth and chin clumsy. Her eyes are fine and
her forehead good. Her hands and arms are well-
made, but not delicate; her figure is full and
shapely. Altogether, a woman.
“Adrian Ross,” writer of librettos for
Gaiety burlesques, is identical with A. R. Ropes,
author of the fine poem “ Ghosts ” reprinted in
The Bulletin three issues back. Which proves
that a poet may also be an able burlesque writer,
but does not prove the converse that a burlesque
writer is the same thing as the poet.
Madame Christian, the popular contralto
vocalist, who not long ago ’verted to the Catholic
Church as a result of Charles Santley’s teaching,
is to leave Melbourne as soon as she takes her
benefit. She will then settle permanently in
Sydney, where several leadings ociety women
have already secured her a promise of many pupils.
A N.S.W. country paper thus cordially
“ sends off ” a departing medico : “ Dash Hyphen
Blank, Esq., M.D., it is said, is about to leave
this district. He came unsolicited. He remained
here uusympathised with and unsympathetic. He
leaves unlamented, and, no doubt, when he gets
away his abuse of the place will be unsparing.”
Cordial.
Sydney Morning Herald having referred
to the “late” Mr. Harwood’s performance of
Uncle Cattermole, the comedian in question took
an early opportunity to assure himself, by looking
in the glass and feeling his mortal frame, that he
was no later than usual. Henry Harwood is alive
also vigorous. He doesn’t remember to have eve,
_ r
felt less dead than at present, and he will not
dream of dissolution without advertising his
decease in the Morning Herald, so that no mis-
take of the same kind may occur again.
Dr. W. H. Dutton, of Melb. divorce
notoriety, intends to keep the Arrowtown (Otago)
Hospital committee to its engagement. He
was selected medical officer from 31 applicants,
and duly appointed, and he maintains that, as he
had done nothing wrong since his appointment,
the committee had no power to cancel the engage-
ment.
Dr. Augustus Mueller, the apostle of the
strychnine antidote for snake-poison, owes his
interest in such researches to the fact that, 35 years
ago, soon after his arrival in Australia, he was
nearly killed by a lively tiger-snake. The doctor,
by the way, is an enthusiastic vigneron, and took
several prizes for wines at the recent Yio. Agri-
cultural Show.
The cherubic Henry Manners-Sutton—
who looks more like F. W. Haddon than ever—
after many trials at many games, has now dropped
into the secretaryship of the New Oxford and
Cambridge Club. A good selection, says The
Bulletin’s London correspondent, for he is a bon
vivant to the very ends of his nails, and can order
a better dinner than most men.
National Ass Willcox, Mayor of Adelaide,
loves to bask in the sunshine of vice-regality.

A “ society ” Sydney daily says that some-
body or other has been entertaining “Lord
Frankfort,” who is at present visiting Australia,
and that Miss Cleopatra Johnson was at the
party. Who is Lord Frankfort? The present
writer has been reading English papers for 25
years, and he has never yet taken up a ’Orne
journal without encountering the name of a lord
he had never before heard of.
A very smarc and wealthy Australian resi-
dent, some years ago, made over most of his
fortune to his son, with an eye to the avoidance of
succession-duty. Then the son died, bequeathing
his father’s pile back to him, and the sagacious
old man had to pay probate and succession-duty
on his own fortune. When he dies another suc-
cession-duty will have to be paid, so the State
gets at that sagacious father twice over.
Dr. Black, Melbourne, is dead, aged 95.
At the original Sydney Crown auction, he bought
one of the Melb. lots, corner Swanston and Col-
lins-streets, when that part was wild forest.
Coming to Melb., his lawyer, who lived in the
township at the west end, told him his lot was
quite out in the country. Dr. Black held this
golden corner, long known as Neave’s buildings,
right up to the present.

Leeston, Canterbury, M.L. In Parliament he
had spoken of the electors in that locality as
“country bumpkins,” and by invitation became
to Leeston town-hall to explain the obnoxious
term away and set thinsrs right. He gave the
bumpkins two hours of “Destiny, Human Race,
Unborn Millions, and Utopia.” A big, lanky
Scot, who spoke with a Bible twang, in a eulogistic
speech proposed the usual hearty of thank B

A PLEA FOR REPUBLICS.
The great standard, and the only true basis of
value throughout the world with all nations, in
Labor. Frossard’s Cavour Cigars are a striking
instance of the fact alluded to, for loyal labor
has originated their good value, in which lies the
secret of their success and universal popularity.
Frossard’s Cavour Cigars are mild aromatic, and
give pleasure to all, while the cost of a packet
being only one shilling puts them within range of
everyone. They are smoked by the millions ; they
are a genuine democratic article originating from
a republican country.
The late Detective Otto Berliner left the
Vic. detective force because of his outspokenness
in the matter of the murder of Margaret Graham
at Daylesford, for which a man named David
Young was executed. Berliner was sent up to
investigate, and made a report in opposition to
that of old “Jack” Williams, who held that
Young was the murderer. Berliner was equally
sure that Young was innocent, and he asserted
that Williams placed the wipe which hanged
Young upon the mantel-shellm Graham’s hut.
Evangelist M ‘Neill has a worldly brother
named Joe living at Sheekago. When the noisy
saint was in that town Joe couldn’t stand it, so he
went for a holiday some 300 miles away. After a
week’s absence M’Neill received a letter, asking
him to forward 25d01., as the exchequer had run
low; but Joe, knowing John’s dilatoriness in
answering letters, enclosed a self-addressed
envelope. Poor John almost fainted when, on
opening that envelope, he found a letter something
after this style “My dear Joe, —I received your
letter all right. Glad to hear you are enjoying
your holidays, and hope to see you back soon. I
enclose the 25d01., as asked. Ever your loving
brother, John.”
An ex-Fort-street school-boy, aged 18
years, paid <£6o and joined the Royal Tar on her
first trip to Paraguay. He got so disgusted with
the constant quarrelling amongst the settlers that
he made up his mind to strike for home again.
Accordingly, he left Villa Rica in December last
and landed in Sydney early in this month, having
travelled 30,000 miles and encountered many ad-
ventures. Carrying his swag through South
America, he did a few weeks’ navvying in the Ar-
gentine, and worked his passage as a deck-hand in
trading-vessels of several nationalities, with a
fixed determination of reaching Australia. Young
Sorensen doesn’t regret his trip, and thinks the
experience well worth £6O.
Another literary buccaneer cheats S. F.
Argonaut of August 6 into printing Brunton
Stephens’verses, “ A Piccaninny,” not only with
authorship unacknowledged, but headed “To a
Papoose,” and preceded by this note :
The following verses were received from the Indian
country, where they were composed by a gentleman
attached to a command there who has the Protean quali-
fications of guide, scout, newspaper correspondent
hunter, and, as will be seen, poet.
The thief omits unsuitable verses, and gets “ local
colour” by introducing a “ Si wash ” and carefully
substituting “ Digger ” for “ nigger.” Is this the
first result of exhibiting a copy of Brunton at the
big Chicago fair ?
The stipendiary man-butcher of a certain
Australian province has several favorite pubs
which he patronises. At some he is served as
other customers, but at one particularly he is
singled out (without his knowledge) for special
treatment. When he first bestowed the honor of
his custom upon this particular hostelry the pro-
prietor made it a rule that any glass used by the
hangman should be at once broken, believing that
it would prejudice business were customers offered
a glass which might by any chance have been used
by the man-slayer. This, however, was an expen-
sive proceeding, so the landlord had a small
cabinet fixed under the counter, in which are kept
one pint measure, one half ditto, a tumbler and a
wineglass, for the sole use of Her Majesty’s finisher
of the law. The gentleman’s favorite tipple is
rum.
Mr, Frederick Young, it is
cabled, has written the London
press that ere the Australias are
asked to contribute directly to the
cost of national defence they must
be granted an equitable share in
controlling the expenditure. How
Mr. Frederick Young aforesaid,
who is always blithering about Aus-
tralian questions, managed to bar-
nacle himself on to this country is a
mystery. He is understood to have
started life as a copper-smith in
“the City,” and, so far as people
here know, has never been to Aus-
tralia in all his life.

lost an opportunity of telling Aus-
tralians that they were musically a

he comes before them. At 1< ast,

land. But he brought this message
to his old confrere, Baron V"ii
Mueller : “ The Academy of Muni h
sends greeting, and 30 years’ re-
membrance to the Humboldt of
Australia.” Baron Yon Mueller,
as'.everyone knows, is modest about
his own achievements, but, “Inde
interests of Art,” he took Hi rr
Moosbriigger to Herr Pahst, hence
Herr M.’s singing at the Risregliato
Society, when everyone thought he
had come out of fairyland. The
contrast between his fresh voice
and his Father Christmas face is so striking that
the whole thing has the air of a tale about it.

N.S.W. Supreme Court Bench —and in retiring

term of 15 years) load the province with yet
another annual pension of .£lBOO. Mr. Foster is
a man who should never have been appointed to
the Bench at all. He is a narrow-minded, well-
meaning gentleman of limited intellect, whom the
Great Architect of the Universe obviously de-
signed as stipendiary president of a Little Bethel.

Patterson told the Oastlemaine electors he
would like to see some religious element introduced
into the State schools. The plate?
Crick, M.P., seeks to make N.S.W. State-
education free. To be justly compulsory, educa-
tion should be both secular and free.
Treas. Carter : “I have seen the Premier
moved to tears over retrenchment.” Yes, over
the Ministerial screws retrenchment.
Teetotal pastor Boyce doesn’t like N.S.W.
Premier Reid, who said nothing in the Governor’s
speech about either Wine or Woman.
There are only four squatters in Q. Legis-
lative Assembly. But they are all members of
the United Pastoralists’ Association.
John M‘Elhone, in meek phraseology and
courtly tenderness, butters the Carruthers Land
Bill in the columns of S.M. Herald. A miracle !
Punch M‘Kinley’s fight with ex-Speaker
Davies, at Toorak, would have been bitter, for it
is understood Mao. regards himself as one of the
choice victims of the Daviesian coterie.

opposed Geo. Turner at last St. Hilda election in
the Asslet interest, this time supported his quon-
dam opponent against Barrister Coldham.
The franchise women in Melb. brought
pressure to bear upon all the candidates for Par-
liament, and extracted pledges in favour of
Women’s Suffrage from the majority of them.
According to public rumor, N.S.W.
Ministers have already begun to foist their rela-
tives on the public service. Perhaps it is all ahe
—in which case the sooner Ministers say so the
better.
Dear Bulletin, —It is but pandering to
the vanity of the “hewers of wood and drawers
ot water ” to talk of them as “ lions led by asses.”
Much more true—“sheep driven by foxes.’
Duncan Maoallum.
The parting of the ways has at last come
between the S.M. Herald and M'Millan. On
the one day, M‘Millan threw in his lot with those
who plot to sell the Sydney trams, and the
Herald resolved to boldly oppose the project.
M'Millan’s conversion to exploiting syndicacy
goes on natural lines, but it shows the world
moves when the Herald stands up boldly to
advocate a front plank in State Socialism.

Why do civil-
servants, right
through Australia,
almost invariably
‘‘vote Conserva-
tive” and despise
the Labor cand l
'dates? It is the Conservative majority which
cuts down salaries to save monopolist lands and
incomes. The civil-servants, pampered in flush
times, are stuck like fatted pigs when State
revenues fall off. The Radical would fly at
higher game and tax property rather than personal
exertion.
Included in the Kingston Govt. (S.AA are
four ex-military men : Sergeant Kingston, Capt.

The day after Labor-member Browne’s
expulsion from Q. Assembly for alleged disorderly
conduct, a Govt, agent hunted him up to say he
could take his seat and steps would be taken to
rescind the suspension. Browne didn’t see it.
The new Adelaide Labour weekly is to
appear early next month. For 10s. a supporter
of the paper is to have as many rights as can
possibly be purchased by the largest shareholder,
so that the editorial staff will have a giddy time.
A young squatocratic scion out Casterton
(Yic.) way tried a joke at an election-meeting.
Thus : “Are you in favah of the State supplying
lunatics with afternoon-tea?” Answer: “I
understand the gentleman wants to get his after-
noon-tea free.”
Jarqes Brown Patterson polled 824 more
than his opponent the last time he appeared
before the Yic. electors. James Brown Pat-
terson, K.C.M.G., comes in a bad second this time.
What’s in a name ? Much depends on “ who gave
you that name?”
Yic. Labor-party didn’t fall into the error
of opposing men like Deakin and Best this
time, with the result that, though according to
Argus classification only 10 Labor-members are
returned, three-quarters of the present House is
solidly Democratic.
Only two Yic. candidates forfeited their
deposits at the general election, and, on the whole,
there was little vote-splitting. Were the Second
Ballot in action only 19 members would be liable
to go to the poll again, and of these a dozen would
be absolutely certain of success.
The Fat Man Legislators who represent
Rockhampton and district replied thusly to a
mass meeting of their constituents which pro-
tested by telegram against the Peace Preservation
Bill —“ We cannot believe our constituents under-
stand the measure.” They evidently cannot believe
that the men who were such asses as to elect them
can understand anything.
Melbourne suburban election incident.
The candidate had been filling ’em up again and
again, and a free and independent elector was
spread out drunk on the footpath under his com-
mittee-room window. A supporter of the other
fellow presently put his head in at the door, and
queried earnestly; “Say, mister, hadn’t you
better take in your sign ? ”
The gagging of Q. Labor members during
the debate on the Coercion Bill was evidently
pre-arranged. It was boasted that Labour mem-
bers would be shut up when Stephens relieved the
Chairman of Committees. Premier Nelson rushed
into the Chamber in his dressing-gown, and never
stopped a moment to enquire into the facts of the
case ; while Speaker Cowley refused to allow the
offending members to say a word in explanation.
The saddest feature of Yic. elections is
the return of the holy Mr. Harper for Bourke
East. The Argus rejoices that he is restored to
the country, as it is sadly in need of financial
advisers. The unblastable fatuity of the Argus
in persistently advertising Harper, whose financial
maunderings are the whole of the plagues oi
Egypt rolled into one, shows how hopeless are its
struggles to get out of its bog of conservatism. It
will never smash its old clay idols unless every-
body about the office dies suddenly one of these
fine mornings. Then the Argus might phoenix
somewhat. A few judicious deaths have been the
making of many papers.

A Chinaman, professing to be a “returned
adonist” who once lived at Oastlemaine, was
refused the privilege of landing by the Melb.
Customs officials the other day, because he did
not “know him man Patterson.” Yet presently
Patterson will be unknown to everyone.
The rejection of Buzacott’s Credit Foncier
Bill, by Q. Council, on a point of order, viz., that
it was a money bill and should have first figured
in the Lower House, is the first black mark
against the ex-editor’s Parliamentary prescience.
Parliamentary practice has been a life-hobby with
Buzacott.

RABBI MOSES: “Yes ; dat’s my brudder geeps der bawnshop.”
REV. HUNGERFIELD (who has a large family and poor salary):
“Dear me. You the Rabbi and your brother a pawn-
broker! A strange difference of taste to be found in the
one family.”
RABBI MOSES: Vell, and vat’s more, he dells me he lives by
making monish out of der Christians."
REV. HUNGERFIELD ( eagerly): “In the holy name of charity, intro-
duce me to so remarkable a man.”
The Victorian Labour-party has actually
captured the electorate of Toorak, once the
Windsor of the moneyooraoy of Cabbagegar-
denopolis, and the old-time shrine of Government
House. M'Kinley, the Melbourne Punch boss,
left such a nasty taste in Toorak’s mouth that
Labor-member Reid easily defeated Langton, the
Heaven-born financier of the calico party.
Sydney Truth has blossomed out as a
supporter of the Reid Govt, This is about the
most lightning volte-face ever executed by a news-
paper. It recalls the suddenness of the Yankee
train-curve where a man in the guard’s-van was
able to light his pipe from the engine fire. Truth,
by the way, says it has “buried the hatchet.
But it carefully displays the Government “ adze.”
A candidate in a North-Eastern Vic.
mining township, without even the proverbial one
idea in his head, had stammered and coughed
through his address, and it was time for questions.
A long, lean miner rose up gradually from his
boots like a snake uncurling and started ; Look
here, mister, do you think, in yer own mind, you re
a fit and proper person to represent this con-
stituency?” The questionee did not go to the
poll.
Parry Okeden, the magistrate despatched
by the Q. Government to suppress agrarian out-
I ages in Central Q., has an escort of 50 picked
troopers, specially chosen for horsemanship, rifle-
shooting, bush knowledge—above all, physique.
Armed with the Peace Preservation Act (Coer-
cion), Okeden and his band will patrol the Western
country. The Thomson, Barcoo, Flinders and
Burdekin watersheds will be the scene of opera-
tions.
Apropos that vote of N.S.W. Assembly
that women should get the franchise at some
unstated future time : Man having made such a
muddle of tilings, woman thinks the time has
come when he should stand down and give her a
show. And, in a vague kind of way, man agrees
with her, but he isn’t quite sure that the right
time has arrived. So he’s going to sit down now
and wait till he’s quite made up his mind on that
point.
Under an old law of Geo. IV., still extant
in Queensland, persons can be heavily punished
for “unlawful assemblage” and a number of other
old-fashioned crimes that are almost forgotten
nowadays. Considering that every camp of union
shearers can be arrested and sent to gaol bodily
for unlawful assembly, and that every individual
“ striker” can be run in for about twelve different
offences in the far-reaching Vagrancy Act, the
new Peace Preservation law is just about three
times more superfluous than it originally appeared.
Its only object is to create a lying impression all
over Queensland, and all over the, world, that the
Australian workman is a revolutionary fury, and
that he must be kept down with a strong hand by
all right-minded lawyers, squatters and auc-
tioneers or else the country will be lost.
Let the Australian Natives’ Association
take this to heart and plume itself thereon.
Tozer and Byrnes (Q.) who have recently covered
themselves with political infamy by the introduc-
tion of the first Coercion Act into Australia are
natives, while nearly all the opponents of that
measure are importations.
The beknighted Patterson’s awful con-
tempt for the press is all “sour grapes.” A
wretched Melb. rag, as dull as two dead men, and
enjoying an extensive circulation amongst its own
staff, has been yelping for the man with the iron
jaw, and Patterson showed his gratitude by filling
it up with page electioneering advts.
Hume: “The practice
of contracting debt will al-
most infallibly be abused in
every government. It would
scarcely be more imprudent
to give a prodigal son a cre-
dit in every banker’s shop in
London, than to empower a
statesman to draw bills on
posterity. Either the nation
must destroy public credit,
or public credit will destroy
the nation.”
Editor BuxtiETiK, —
Does anybody remember the
murder of Police-sergeant
Wallings near Dubbo (N.S.
Wales) about 15 years ago?
Perhaps not, but it points a
moral. Wallings and two
constables rode out to a road-
side pub. to arrest a fugitive.
When they arrived they saw
their man crossing a paddock
behind the house. He carried
a-revolving rifle, as was after-
wards learned. Wallings
drew his revolver, and rode
towards him, calling on him
to surrender. The man went
down on one knee and took
a deliberate aim at Wallings,
who rode round him trying
to get a chance of a shot.
The man fired and shot
Wallings dead. Now, if
Wallings had been armed
with a sword, or even a
heavy stick, instead of that
miserable pistol, he would
have simply headed his horse
straight for the kneeling
man at the best pace attain-
able and ridden him down.
But the man would have
fired all the same. Probably,
but the horse’s head and
neck would have shielded
the rider, even if the sight
of a horseman bearing dow r n
on him had not disordered
his aim. The chance of the
horse being shot dead is not
worth considering. There
are about 10,000 chances to
one against a shot directly in
front bringing down a horse.
It must hit him in one spot
so small that it could be
covered with a half-crown.
It was really his own re-
volver that killed Wallings.
—> Trooper.

maine (Yic.) district were religiously attended by
the local police. When the opponents of the
blatant butcher were numerically weak and in-
dividually old and infirm, the Ministerial “,copr
pers ” ejected them for interrupting “Sir James,’’
It is not legal for the force to remove a man who
openly criticises an election candidate whilst he
is speaking, bub Patterson’s official chuckers-oujt
professed to be maintaining lawanorder, and these
solemn outrages were perpetrated with impunity
—in the bush.
Melb, Aegus, having failed miserably to
win Victoria to Freetrade with puerile texts on
stupid little flag pictures, and puerile verses, and
absolutely idiotic leading articles, had the astound-
ing gall to publish, on the morning after the
battle, a banner bearing the strange device, “The
New House is pledged to Tariff Beform,” This
childish work of AKGUS fiction put the Tory
journal beyond the pale of intelligent pity. In
truth, even Fat Man is sick of his silly old organ,
now that it has given up all idea of catching the
general public (which rather frightened Fat Man
for a time) and become a mere penny ghost of its
old self. The twopenny Argus contained heavy
literary matter of some merit, but as a penny-
sheet trying to live by it i ordinary daily intelli-
gence it is a ghastly production.
The Argus pretends to gloat over the 20th
as a Freetrade victory. A few more such victories !
&c. The Protectionist cause had all the dramatic
effects this time. Graham Berry, Alfred DeaUin,
H. B. Hiergins, and George Turner came in with
a dash the devil himself would not lie about as
the Abgus does. The foreign-trade pets, McCrac-
ken and Cold ham, were completely dished.
Murray Smith got only 39 majority over a very
weak opponent, and that after the most marvellous
Tory canvass ever known in Victoria. Atßarvvon,
Levien would have been erased by an entirely new
man, Crouch, if six votes had been cast the other
way. A Liberal ousts the typical Freetrader,
Langton, at Toorak !!! Hancock’s 800 majority
made 10,000 people in Oollins-st., that night, sick
with delight. Major Smith and Francis Long-
more come back to Parliament to celebrate a
Freetrade victory in a House containing 00 per
cent of Protectionists.

JL ..C other day the writer, while passing a
/arge and eminently respectable - looking city
butchery in Sydney, was gratified by the spectacle
of a fat man licking price-labels with his tongue
and sticking them on the carcases. He put the
label well back in his mouth, as if he was going to
swallow it, moistened it thoroughly, and fixed it
on, and then he slapped the dead body with his
hand to make the ticket stick. When be had done
this about a dozen times The Bulletin man sud-
denly felt bad, and passed on. The meat-trade is
probably the dirtiest one going. The butcher’s
window is left unglazed, so that all the dust of
creation may blow in. The carcases are hung out-
side, and dogs come and scratch themselves
against them, and small boys whack them play-
fully as they go by, and skinny women feel them
to decide whether the quality is up to the mark.
And when the ticket horror is added to all the
rest it is too much.
* * *
A Yic. parson lectured, the other day, on
the question, “Is woman cleaner than man ? ”
After he had walked round the problem in all
directions, and considered the cleanness and the
dirtiness of both sexes from every possible point
of view, he summed up, in effect, that he didn’t
know whether woman was cleaner than man or
not. The only visible excuse for the lecture is
that the doubtful cleanliness of women drew a
3 arge audience.
* # *
Dear Bulletin, —Re this double-name
craze, here’s a fact. A Sydney man who happens
to have a stylish intermediate name was lately
told by his sister-in-law that she wished she had
been his wife, because then, instead of plain (say)
Jones, she could have been Mrs. (say) Lancaster-
Jones —“ with a hyphen, you know ! ” These self-
hyphened snobs must be fools, as well, not to
know that the caste with which they pant to
assimilate themselves is moved only to august
laughter by any combination not upon the brief
list of inherited compound names, some Australian
bearers of which, by the way, have actually
retrenched their patronymics lest they should be
confounded with people who try in this way to
“imply a grandfather.”— Montmokenci Robin-
son-Jones.

Ladies who fancy to improve their com-
plexions with arsenic have one or two things to
remember. Used for long, it very often produces
a very ugly, bladdery-looking eruption ( eczema
arsenicale). Also it very often “turns dog” on
the user, and pigments the skin a dirty brown in
patches. Also if much of your complexion falls
into your tea you have a fair show of falling into
your coffin.

Dear Bulletin, — Be your par. last week
about the whilom Tummy. I, as a fat man fast
getting a waist, would testify to the efficacy of the
thyroid tabloid as a reducer of lymphatic fat. I
was 20st. five weeks ago—l am now 18st. 81bs.
iiy
taking three tabloids daily I lose an average
of 41b. or so a week. The only apparent ill-effects
are a slight nervousness and loss ot appetite.—
Anti-Sylph.

The average “trap” has truly a strange
sense of humor. During the recent trial of a
Kanaka for murder, one of Her Majesty’s peace
preservers amused himself at the expense of the
unhappy nig. by pointing to an ominous beam
above the dock. The P.M. who presided, oblivi-
ous of the silent tragedy which was being enacted,
tried in vain to understand the secret of the poor
wretch’s trembling - fits; but by the time the
islander, who could not speak a word of English,
reached the higher court, his raven looks were
streaked with grey.
* # •
What competition and the low price of
flour can do for the poor ! An Adelaide baker is
advertising two loaves of bread for Id. Other
bakers are selling the same weight of bread at
from l|d. to 5d., according to the complacency of
their customers.
# • •
The splendid tin-pottery of N.S. Wales
military management is well shown by the occa-
sional apparition of a small box-cart, drawn by
four horses, which makes its way from Paddington
barracks to a suburban produce store, about half-
a-inile off, and returns with a load of forage. The
cart is attended by from one to three men beside
the driver. People desiring to produce a very
small result with a very large expenditure of
means should carefully study Sydney military
methods.

Yellow Aster,” and the library lists are all 50
deep. Some young ladies openly declare that
Gwendolen’s sentiments don’t strike them as un-
natural at all —that, in fact, she is no “Yellow
Aster,” but just the common or garden variety.
Maturer females (pace “ Faustine who isn’t a
female ?) consider the indelicacy lies in the shame-
less blurting-out of a girl’s inmost feelings, but

Sunday newspapers have long been a well-
established fact in Sydney. Yet they work under
one great legal disadvantage— viz., that anyone
who gets credit for advts. or for papers supplied
can repudiate the debt on the ground of the
illegality of the publication. This has actually
been done by several inexpressibly mean people.

The latest dodge of the Melb. demi-monde
is to canvass business offices and warehouses in
the garb of hospital nurses, and leave their cards
lying round in some conspicuous position.
m * •
A correspondent writes asking Jerome K.
Jerome about the advisability of emigrating to
Victoria. And Jerome answers him to the effect
that Victoria is a “ wild, lonely country,” and
that the risks of going out into that horrible wil-
derness are very great.
# # •
Stipendiary Addison, of Sydney, theothe;
day punished a publican, convicted of selling as
brandy a mixture of white spirit which analyst
Hamlet testified was poisonous, with a fine of £5
and £2 2s. costs. But if a man sells good spirits
without a license he will be fined £50 —as many
have been fined. It therefore follows that it is
ten times worse to defraud the Customs than to
poison the community.
# # #
The latest suggestion made by the Shriek-
ing Sisterhood, that domestic servants shall be
required to wear a distinguishing livery, would,
if established, abolish at one swoop the Genteel
Order of Lady Helps. Thus, female snobocracy,
before it can gain its point, has to fight the luck-
less amongst the aristocracy as well as the whole
body-social of servantgaldom. Wherefore, its fad
is still-born.
w * «
T. A. Westwood, the first of the original
New Australia pilgrims to leave Lane, returned
to Adelaide some weeks ago, and has now gone to
Ooolgardie. He asserts that Lopez, Paraguayan
Minister of the Interior, altered the telegram sent
to England by Dr. Stewart, British Minister at
Asuncion, so as to make it appear that only eight
persons had left the settlement instead of 83.
Otherwise he thinks that the second contingent of
the Royal Tar’s passengers would have backed
out, for she lay in Adelaide waters for some days
after the receipt of the warning telegram from the
Secretary of State last December. Westwood
says several New Australians are so hard-up
that they will never be able to get even as far as
lluenos Ayres without assistance from here.
# • *
Some evidence given recently in a Christ-
church (M.L ) assault and battery case, furnishes
a lot of pleasant reflections about the ways of the
s ie-witness in her irresponsible moments. The
plaintiff, an almost unrecognisable mass of
bandages and sticking-plaster, testified, with
extreme reluctance, and under great pressure, that
she was the unmarried wife of the defendant, and
that, on a certain evening, they met in a quiet
locality, and he gave her a drink of lemonade.
Then he proceeded, for no visible reason, to pound
her on the head with the bottle until she was
nearly dead. When he had finished pounding her
lie repented and apologised, and carried the
battered form of his mistress tenderly home.
There finding that it was necessary to get medical
assistance and to offer some explanation of what
had happened, they sent for the police, and
the girl laid an information for assault against
another man, while her assailant corrobo-
rated the statement, and told how he
had rescued her from the brute in ques-
tion, and hauled him off his prostrate victim.
Somehow the arrangement fell through later on,
probably because the other man had too con-
clusive an alibi, so he didn’t get the two years’
gaol intended for him. But the plaintiff acknow-
ledged quite calmly, and with a lingering pathos
in her voice, that she still loved the man who
staved her head in with the bottle, and out of her
affection for him she had tried to get the other
man into gaol instead. She had no animosity
against the other man, but she recognised that the
police wouldn’t believe she had stoushed her own
head with the bottle, and, therefore, he had to be
sacrificed. She was so unaffectedly sorry that he
hadn’t been sacrificed, and so absolutely impassive
about it, that it was plainly no good lecturing her
on the utter sinfulness of her conduct, so the
court only sighed inwardly and made a mental
note that a woman in love was of no value as a
witness.
> # * *
A Melb. paper tells a romantic story of a
man who, some twelve years ago, lost his sight as
the result of a gun-accident. His case was
regarded as hopeless, and he settled down to spend
the rest of his life in darkness. About five years
after the disaster, he fell in love with a girl—
probably because her voice was musical and
sympathetic, and her face and hair seemed at-
tractive to his touch —and they were married, and
lived happily until a few weeks ago. Then a
Melb. oculist undertook to operate on his eyes and,
to the surprise of the patient and everyone who
knew him, his sight was restored, and he saw his
wife for the first time. Whether they are living
happily now, or whether their peaceful home is
exploded in many pieces, isn’t known. The feelings
of a man, when he sees his wife for the first time
after eight years of married life, are probably
rather mixed. The man who marries a wife in
the dark takes a serious risk, and the one who,
having done that, looks at his wife in the daylight,
is a gambler of the most reckless description.
* * *
Still another development in the Te Kloot
(Sydney) divorce-case. Te Kloot applied for and
obtained a rule nisi in divorce with .£2OOO
damages against the influential and well-connected
co-respondent, which damages were ordered to be
settled on the faithless wife as a reward for her
infidelity. Then Te Kloot, an unfortunate Govern-
ment clerk at a microscopic salary (who had been
treated in a most rascally manner by his official
superiors), was ordered to pay his own and his
wife’s costs, which everyone was aware he couldn’t
possibly do. Finally, when he applied to have
the divorce made absolute, the court decided that
he was in “contempt” for non-payment of, or
rather inability to pay, the said costs, and couldn't
have his decree until he settled up, so he remains
in a partly-marred condition which is not very
definable. Next he will doubtless get into gaol
for his “ contempt,” and by-and-bye it will prob-
ably appear that being only partly unmarried he
is still liable for alimony. Truly there is one law
for the rich and 1G others, that-the rich never hear
of, for the poor.

Ab Christchurch (M.L.) Cathedral, three
Sundays ago, Parson Paseoe, holding forth on the
new Maori land Divorce Bill, said: “If the law
were altered so that women could obtain divorces
from their husbands on the same grounds as tho.-e
for which husbands can obtain divorces from their
wives, it would leave the door open for the disso-
lution of half the marriages in Christchurch.”
Then every woman in the congregation looked at
her husband with the expression of one whose
ideal was shattered, and there was a silence in
which the breathing of an .ant would have been
distinctly audible.
* * *
Corporal punishment in N.S.W. State-
schools is inflicted only in extreme cases, and each
case is recorded in a book kept for the purpose. A
certain inspector has a funny little way of asking
all scholars chastised on the day preceding his
visit to rise in their places. A comparison of the
number with the number recorded is generally
disastrous to the record. The same ingenious
examiner overhauls the blotting-pad to ascertain
if the attendance is marked morning and evening.
A man like this is lost in the Education Depart-
ment. He should join the Bradawl Brigade.

Dear Bulletin, — Be your constant stric-
tures on honorary J’s.P. An amazonian N.S.W.
State school-teacher exercised her muscle on a
pupil. The local School Board investigated, and
reported unfavourably. The parent issued a
summons and she was hauled before a police-court,
found guilty, and fined. Here I introduce the
J.P. Two members of the Bench were also mem-
bers of the School Board that had already investi-
gated. Naturally prejudiced against her—for
they had to find her guilty or eat their report —
they hadn’t the manliness to vacate the Bench
and leave the Stipendiary to deal with the case.
A side-light is thrown by the fact that a son of
one member had been severely caned, and has
since been expelled from the same school.— Milog.
* * *
The Yankee notion of a “ surprise wed-
ding” has been forced upon the attention of
Melbourneites for the first time. In the
earlier days of the present month a number of
friends were invited to dinner at a certain
mansion, and while they were still hanging about
for the signal of attack on the viands provided,
the young bride and her aged lover—old enough,
at any rate, to be her father—issued from their
lair of flowers and foliage, attended by the neces-
sary parson. Some of the local papers state that
the guests were thoroughly astonished and after-
wards overjoyed ; but, as a matter of fact, every-
body knew of the approaching marriage, which
had been announced in print weeks previously.
For the present all that we want is a “sur-
prise christening.” Gorger, a Toorak mag-
nate, is determined to try it when next he
becomes a father, which he calculates will be on
The Modern Way

Neuralgia
is the first, surest and most reliable sign of nerve
decay; it is a symptom provided by nature to
warn the sufferer of approaching weakness and
distress. Though in most cases the pain is purely
local, the disease is constitutional; and local
remedies, such as liniments, oils and menthols,
only give temporary relief and do not do the
slightest permanent good, for they only act by
deadening the sensibility of the affected nerve.
The remedy, to be of any lasting benefit, must be
tissue-forming and nerve-strengthening, and must
have general invigorating properties ; it must also
contain the material to restore the lost tissue to
the impoverished nerves, and no remedy except.
Clements Tonic possesses these wonderful powers.
Clements Tonic has been used for thousands of
oases by young and old of both sexes for neuralgia,,
sciatica, and other nervous complaints, and we
have never heard of a case where it has failed to.
give quick and prompt relief. Read of the case of
Mrs. T. Watts, Woonona, N.S.W., who writes om
March 23rd, 1893 I have suffered severely from,
indigestion for the last three or four years. I was;
unable to sleep, and had a continual languid!
feeling, loss of appetite, severe pains in the chest ,
and stomach, and was half of my time unable to>
perform my household duties with any degree of.
pleasure. I had the advice and medicine of severall
doctors, but without receiving any benefit. Hear-
ing Clements Tonic so highly spoken of for the
above complaint, I resolved to give it a trial. I
felt myself improving from the very first dose, and!
am happy to say that before I had finished th«!
first bottle all disagreeable symptoms had diaap>-
peared, and I felt better than I had done foar
years; I have never had a return of the old com-
plaint since. I recommend it on all occasions.—I
remain, yours gratefully, Mrs. T. Watts, Woo-
nona, N.S.W.

• * *
The other day a pale, anxious-looking
little boarding-house keeper was passing through
one of the Sydney parks when she saw a large
man on a seat, and recognised him as a boarder
who had fled some time previously, leaving his
bill behind him. He didn’t notice her, so she
attracted his attention by “ fetching him a
woodener” on the ear with her umbrella. Then
he got up with a yell and charged at her, and she
have him a whack which sent his hat flying
36f. While he was recovering from this she
whirled the umbrella round horizontally and
let him have an altogether surprising shock on
the jaw. Just here the ex-boarder gathered him-
self up and ran for his life, getting a prod in the
coat-tails as he started, and the little woman ran
after him, but was presently distanced. As she
bolted round a corner she collided with the flying
debtor’s brother and seized him. “ Where does
Tom live now,” she said, excitedly; “tell me,
for I’m going to get my money or know the reason
why.” “I won’t tell you,” said the brother,
doggedly. Then there was a biff that might have
been heard in tho next suburb, and a stunned
brother was sitting on a flattened hat in the
roadway, with a 7st, woman standing over him in a
threatening attitude. A large park official came
along at this moment and laid a hand on the in-
dignant female. “Just you go along out of this
quietly, ma’am,” he said, “or you’ll get into
trouble, you know.’’ “Oh, yes, I’ll get along
quietly enough so long as you don’t touch me.
Take your hand off me now, or I’ll break your
head with the umbrella.” And she whirled the
gamp with such dreadful precision that the official
backed hurriedly and contented himself with
walking behind her as far as the gate. That is the
only kind of woman who can run a boarding-house
and keep out of the insolvency court these times,
and even she doesn’t get more than a bare living.
The only woman who will ever make a hash-house
pay really well is a woman who can run like
Atalanta, and who always goes after the defaulting
boarder with a hatchet.

and tatters of power. One of the Yic. judges was
passing a warehouse in Swanston-street, when a
porter came out, humping his sack, and bumping
his Honor, with the remark —‘ Look out, mate !
Judge ; ‘ Who are you calling mate ? I have a
good mind to complain to your employer.’ Re-
minds me of what I saw in Little Collins-street.
At the back of a swell place, a van was all across
that narrow thoroughfare. A drayman remon-
strated. Out came a bumptious personage, with
‘How dah you, fellow? If you are insolent, I
shall repaht you to your employah.”

The Saturday classes held at Fort-street,
Sydney, for the alleged benefit of metropolitan
State pupil-teachers, are to end at last, after some
40 years’ existence. Notices have been received by
the visiting masters and mistresses informing
them that after the end of the year their
services will no longer be required, as the
classes are then to be discontinued. The
province will save a vast sum by this wise
step. Each visiting master or mistress gets i>2s
per annum for one lesson of half-an-hour’s dura-
tion. But the education authorities won’t have it
that retrenchment is the object in view. One
high official avers that the main objection to eon-
tinuing the classes is the immorality of bringing
together so large a body of young people of both
sexes. Forty years is rather a long while to take
to study the moral aspect of the classes.

The Col-' 1 ■ e of the con-
gregation makes all the differ-
ence. A coasting captain
reached the bar of a Northern
river, the other day, and
noted with disgust that the
channel had shifted. A black-
coat was hovering anxiously
around, so the captain court-

“ You’d better go below for a

Opposition coaches run between Condobo-
lin and Forbes since the Wyalong (N.S.W.) gold-
rush. The other day the two coaches were at the
Condobolin end, awaiting return passengers to
Forbes. There was only a swagman. A fierce
struggle to ensnare him took place between the
rival Jehus. It ended in a “dead-heat.” One
coachee bundled in the man ; the other collared
the swag. And off went the coaches twain.
Arrived at Forbes, the swaggie had no cash
wherewith to pay the fare —spoke of being kid-
napped, and talked largely about damages-at-law.
Also of criminal proceedings anent the stealing of
a swag. Result: Liquor, apologies, and a trifle
to “see you farther on your road, mate.”

LAW OF LIEN.
Last week, at Sydney, under writ of habeas corpus,
Justice Foster enforced restitution to a mother of her
infant, detained by another woman as security for pay-
ment of its maintenance; he farther ruled that the
clothes it wore must (to with the child; and remarked
that the law did not provide for liens on babies.
“Have ’is carcase ” redivivus,
Tho’ these hairs so few and gray be,
Our posterity survive us—
Tuck thine aegis round the baby !
Loquitur presiding Justioe :
“ What, ma’am, could your reason pray be?
Sobbeth answer : “ Wrong’d my trust is,
For its keep I claim the baby.”
“Part,” quoth Law, “ that infant’s corpus
She : “ The duds I bought, my prey be.”
Law rejoins : “ No ruth can warp us ;
Clothes are parcel of the baby.
“ Need is clear for public stating.
Mortgaged wool and lambkin may be;
But we still lack forms creating
Valid lien on swaddled baby.”

Harrison White’s “Slumber Song.” Second

“I Was Dreaming’’ (Juncker). 11,000 copies
sold. Fifth edition ready.
Leon Caron’s “Nitouche Galop,” admired by
pianoforte players. Go from start to finish.
Everyone is anxious to hear Miss Nellie Stewart
sing “ 1 Was Dreaming ” in “ Ma Mie Rosette.”
H. J. Samuell’s musical publications are very
much liked. He is bringing out something fresh.
Talmage has dashed across the Australian
sky like a patent Yankee meteor, a nine-days
wonder of which the tenth day finds no trace. He
denounces drink, of course, but only for the reason
that he has found a better substitute. “ When I
feel out of sorts,” ho told a startled interviewer,
“ when gorgons and hydras and chimaeras come
gibbering along the nerve-tracks of this wonderful
body of mine, and variegated serpents curl and
twist along the corridors of this magnificent brain,
it is then that I call for Mrs. T, to unlock her
little cupboard and take down from the top shelf
that great modern restorative, infinitely preferable
to alcohol —the nectar of the eternal, Nature’s own
panacea. Marshall’s Tonic Nervine.
