My Dear Moorabinda—
Mrs. Annie Besant (pronounced Bezant on the
final authority of the Most-Travelled) made her
first bow to a Sydney audience at the local Opera
House last Tuesday under thetegis of Judge Win-
deyer. What most struck me in “ the most won-
derful woman of the nineteenth century ” was her
absolute naturalness, for I really believe the rose-
pinkness of her ears was caused by excitement
and not grease-paint, as an uncharitable person
used to the ways of actresses might be forgiven
fur thinking. In figure Mrs, Besant is not just as
straight as a bulrush, and her shapely hands are
tough-looking and sinewy. Despite a white silk
negligee ruffled with tea-coloured lace, you couldn’t
call the speaker graceful—she is too nervo-fibrous
to give a thought to posing, and doesn’t even
smile prettily when the crowd applauds, but
waits with a grim stoicism until the noise ceases.
1 don’t suppose Mrs. Besant sets much value on
the encouraging cheers of mobs, since, not long
ago, the whole world combined to hoot her.
I never heard anyone talk so well as Mrs.
Besant—whose voice and manner, by the way, are
here and there strongly reminiscent of Mrs,
Brough. She has the advantage of convincing
earnestness, and the various nuances of feeling are
so faithfully expressed that even a naked heathen
would know when to drop a silent tear and when
to show his teeth. For women, Mrs. Besant is
quite a little “East Lynne” on wheels. When
she gave pathetic particulars about baby match-
box-makers, so much audible sniffling and flutter-
ing of handkerchiefs among the women resulted
that she might have been Lady Isabel weeping
over her little Willie.
But if Mrs. Besant weren’t miles above material
things, I would most respectfully advise her to
take a few lessons in conventional stage deport-
ment, or else come on with a fan or something to
support herself by. The most wonderful woman
of her time has got her stage-legs, apparently, but
she hasn’t got her stage hands yet. She wears a
huge signet-ring—that, I am told, of the late
Mine. Blavatsky—and hangs on to it when she
is tired measuring from her nose to her chin with
her forefinger and nursing her left hand, which
she keeps at full point like a sign-post; but a lady
holding herself up by her jewellery doesn’t look
poetical behind the footlights. Mrs. Besant,’s
jewellery, by the way, is of a decidedly spook-like
character. A plain gold ring on the middle finger
of the right hand, the heavy seal on the left, and
about her neck a heavy necklace of round green
stones culminating in a little charm. When Mrs.
B. left the stage in her white gown and shawl and
her white hair she might either have been a dis-
embodied spirit or one of W. & M.’s living
statues (with a little more on) getting away from
her pedestal.
A tremendous crowd turned up at Mrs. Besant’s
first appearance. Ex-Fremiei Parkes listened
with every hair of his head while she deprecated
compulsory education—just as though he hadn’t
flooded the land with clerks in paper-collars and
cotton-backed governesses "with his Education-
while-you-wait Act, or whatever it is. I think
that some of the great audience were attracted by
the hope that the at-one-time interdicted authoress
might go close to the knuckle in her platform
remarks—but their disappointment must have
been keen when Mrs. B. took them over the
slummy side of London in generalising hops!
There was really some disappointment in certain
uninformed circles over the clever and magnetic
lecturess—whom I could' listen to for ever, pro-
vided I had a bun in my pocket. For instance, I
really heard a maiden pouting to her mother : “I
thought she was a Theosophist, but she never
made a cup fly! ” Evidently this ingenious
damsel imagined that the “Dangers which
threaten Society ” consisted of sudden and
unexpected flights of crockery-ware 1
Metrop. Day, at Rand wick, so long ago as
Tuesday week, must be set down as far and away
the best-groomed day at the A.J.C, Meeting.
There was a spanking show of apparel, and, naming
no names, a good-looking woman, tall and well-
formed, with dark hair and a splendid carriage,
simply walked away from the field in the ladies’
race. Her gown was mauve silk with the bodice
and full sleeves of mauve accordeon-pleated
chiffon. A sash falling in front of white
with a suggestion of black jet at either end, and a
black toque with three black feathers hanging off
at the back (somewhat suggestive of an ostrich’s
tail, I must confess) completed the get-up.
The Y.R. box was a long perspective of upper-
crust. Lady Hopetoun, as straight in the profile
as ever, though still looking pale (perhaps through
the nervous strain of being in the company of two
ladies respectively dressed in pink and blue silk
and tumbled gauze), wore a small pink checked
silk with a geranium-pink front barred with black
lace and a black hat with original garniture, and
presented the appearance of a noble sportswoman
who had been backing seconds all day. Lady
Duff wore mushroom-colored brocade of suburban
cut, made with aggravations of mushroom-satin
veiled (everything is as densely, deeply veiled now
as the girl with the painted face) with chiffon.
Also, Lady Duff has repented of her weird
millinery and taken to small bonnets with most
excellent results. Miss Norman’s plain frock was
of violet-checked material. Gov. Duff, in pale
grey, even to his kid gloves, and with a saffron rose
in his buttonhole, flaunted a snowy handkerchief as
if waiting for some beauty to throw it to a (a
‘Morocco Bound,” and a blonde A.D.O. was
chained to the side of a very pretty girl in art
green and a wide hat.
Mrs. Hatton was all in white, like Lot’s wif®
after the salt act. Miss Granville wore the color B
of diplomacy—black and gold. A society madam
in dark green, with appearances of pink, rather
reminded you of a tapped sugar-melon; and I saw a
stray man from Melbourne, who knew not his
Sydney, wink a wicked wink at a “ society ’’ girl
in bright cerulean blue and the sort of hat Mary
Ann wears at Chowder Bay pavilion. I thought
that inconsiderate wink was quite an eloquent
her Sunday frock in particular and
giddy-looking Spring fashions in general.
Judging by the financial success of the Hos-
pitals ball the other Wednesday, the committee
will probably keep to their threat of making an
annual of it. If they always bill it in the full-
blast of race-week it will inevitably repeat its
inaugural success. Decorations of evergreen
garlands, bunting and faint lilies, with here and
there a red cross like a Good Friday bun, made
the hall look really festive, and cosy corners and
tea - and - coffee buffets left no bare spot
under the balconies. Another factor of suc-
cess was the absence of sword-dances and
other variety-turns, which no dancing-girl
aches to stand on one foot and look at; floor
and music were as smooth as silk, and a poultry-
supper at an early hour (vice-regal movements
regulate supper-time, en parenthese; when vice-
regal folks go early the feast is spread before
eleven) was daintily set out in the vestibule and
decorated in a very tasteful way. The vice-regal
programmes (designed by Dr. Mailler-Kendall)
were in the shape of surgical bandages, .and must
have occasioned the convalescent Lady Hopetoun
an unpleasant twinge of memory, I should think.
Lady Hopetoun wore a simple dinner-dress of
mulberry faille with a velvet bodice and a diamond
star in her dark hair. Her Ex., of Victoria also
clutched a weedy handful of daffodils which
looked as if she had plucked them in passing, and
if that is the latest posey there is hope for every-
one with six square feet of flower-bed. The Earl
of H. (whom any lady novelist would call “pale
and distinguished-looking ”) sported a 16-hands
collar in addition to other articles of apparel, and
Gov. Duff wore his new ribbon. But an air of
inconsequent dressing hung over the vice-regal
group, who had been gotten up like butterflies at
the Government House ball the night before, and
there were not enough diamonds to make a caudle
look snuffed.
The most elegant gown which came within my
range of vision was worn by Mrs. S. A. Moss—a
Paris confection of floral brocade, strawberry-
coloured flowers and stripes on a cream ground,
the bodice with a deep shoulder-cape of cream
satin embroidered in gold and made with long
sleeves to the wrist and ruffles of old lace. A mili-
tary lad} 7 , in a bright yellow gown witti her dark
hair insufficiently poudre, reminded you (about
the coiffure, at least) of Mrs. Besant; and a plump
society matron in shiny beads looked like a new
sort of bug. Taking it altogether, the dressing at
the Hospitals’ ball was the weakest part of a very
festive shovy. Being a horribly official occasion,
all the military sparks were crammed into their
short shell jackets, which made Captain Gingah
look as if he had grown out of his clothing. But
since “ Uncle ” has absorbed all the tinsel braid
for his own costume, the home-grown warrior
looks a very unimpressive object indeed—some-
thing like a lory parrot which has moulted its
tail
Sydney Govmentouse burst forth into two big
ceremonious balls during race-week, and both
were favored with fine weather. The present
vice-regalities are rather lucky in the matter of
the weather, and rain seldom keeps their guests
indoors trampling on the carpets all the time. I
am told that both balls were a success, which is
mere repetition, since the very fact of being under
the Y.R. roof is enough for Potts Point; though
there were the familiar complaints about the
wrong people going to the first ball. By the
way, a glance down the invitation-lists shows
that the severe Government House clique of
other days must be scattered to the four winds.
I went to hear the boy soprano, Cyril Tyler (the
name of Cyril fits that boy like a silk stocking),
the other evening, and the question I was asking
myself all the time was—what will happen to the
prodigy-show when they put the prodigy into
pants? Cyril, despite his knickerbockers and
Tommy Merton collar, is getting a big boy now ;
he turned 14 (so they say) while in Sydney, and
loses a head-note or two every year. If this sort
of thing is allowed to go on there will soon be not
enough left of the “angelic” little he-soprano
to advertise with. A hulking youth, with a voice
like a cross-cut saw and a fixed determination to
have his pretty hair cut, will be all that is left of
Cyril. At present, however, he is altogether too
lovely for this world. There is on view a picture
of him taken in a surplice, and with the music of
“We Praise Thee, 0 Lord !” in his hands. When
you gaze upon that beatific vision, with the light
shining glorywise on its elaborately-barbered
locks, it never occurs to you, unless you chance
to be a very coarse person indeed, that front-seats
are four shillings.
But perhaps they’ll treat the nicely-Nancyfied
Master Cyril as the horsey people do the ponies,
give him medicated gin to keep him permanently
in the realms of knickerbockers. Even then, if his
screechy voice follows the natural course of all
boys’ voices, the spectacle of a curly-haired cherub
singing in tones akin to the dulcet strains of the
gentleman who offers you 16 bee-nanas in a bag,
will not attract the tribute of nations. Thank
heaven, I haven’t to go through this vale of tears
with a prodigy for my stock-in-trade.
As a soprano vocalist Master Cyril Tyler (who,
in spite of his painted cheeks and an effeminate
gold ring on his “ engaged ” finger, is almost
distinctly a boy) couldn’t fill a tent if he warbled
in long hair and petticoats, for the marvel of his
singing is that it is exactly like a woman’s voice
proceeding out of a curly-haired boy. His man-
nerisms, too —the half-closed eyes, the seductive
smile behind which your average soprano artfully
conceals the fact she is reaching all she knows for
a top-note—are both woman-y, but then he learnt
’em at his mother’s knee, so to speak, for Mrs.
Tyler has a very fresh soprano voice, and her son
has caught her mannerisms (which are, after all,
only mechanisms) to aT. Both Mrs. Tyler (who
travels with her miracle and is a most charming
personality) and Master TVW hail from the Land
of Wooden Hams, and I really must say that it i*
an evil day for Australia that they ever left it.
Because the evil that a prodigy does lives afte
him. Especially a prodigy who has his boudoir
photo, taken in an angelic surplice with an open
psalter in his hand and his eyes turned up with
the devout look of a duck in a thunderstorm, and
then sticks them—l mean the photos.—in every
shop-window on the Block. A posey picture like
that incites every fond mamma whose offspring has
curly hair to grow her monster, so to speak, and
if we aren’t at once flooded out with attitudinising
brats diddling or trickling about on the piano and
squeaking operaticisms d la Tyler, then the
Fauntleroy boom, when every commonplace child
was trained to call his fat and stertorous mama
‘‘dearest,” is no criterion. If Cyril of the odd-
sized voice—which isn’t half so reedily angelic as
the average little freckle-faced chorister’s who
doesn t “get took” in his vestments —if this
Master Cyril came and went and left no after-
math one might receive him as stoically as one
suffers the two-headed calf and other abnormal
things. Why wasn’t the boy-soprano also a freak
on four legs ? Even the proudest parent wouldn’t
want to imitat ethat. Yours truly, Sappho Smith.
Melbourne Confidences.
Melbourne, September 25.
The Urso-Sapio visit gave the Austral Salon a
needed thrill. Thelionesses, after themannerof their
kind, turned up late, keeping everyone on tenter-
hooks, especially the vivacious French hostess, who
aged 10 years in an hour. Alarmists of the “ 1-do-
hope nothing’s-happened ! ” sort are as consoling
to a foreboding heart as those Melbourne girls
who lately, when their dearest friend’s wedding
was postponed, went about saying, “Now that
he’s swerved once, she’ll never get him to face the
flag again ! ”
However, on this occasion, all that tact could de-
vise was done to lighten the suspense. Tea and cake
diverted the attention of Job’s comforters, and a
young lady was put up to sing “ When we meet!’’
an artful allusion to joys in store. When the guests
at last appeared, they were headed straight for a
pair of capacious divan chairs, just the sort of thing
that a young ladies’ work party gives the bachelor
parson for his study, since slippers dropped out of
date. The guardiags of these sacred seats had
nobly earned their afternoon tea, and that’s a
fact. If they nodded for a moment, exhausted
dowagers jumped the claim and looked hurt when
requested as politely as possible to pick up their
parasols and walk. Alter all, the celebrities
declined the thrones, just as Sara Bernhardt did
at Mr. George Coppin’s historic Sunday-night,
and had to be provided for by the hurried eviction
of two adjacent seat-holders.
A patriarchal doctor now ascended the platform
to announce that the Advent was accomplished,
though, had a blind mute been present, he could
have guessed as much from the sudden ferocity
with which his neighbors jostled him and trampled
on his toes. A long, hard stare ensued, and it was
generally agreed that, seen at close quarters,
Mdme. LTrso is the quaintest and snuggest little
dame possible. Her bunny face, beaming through
a white complexion-veil, was surmounted by a
head-dress exactly like the doll’s bonnet achieved
by juvenile milliners. A round of cardboard,
neatly covered with black silk, dotted with pink
rosettes, strings to match, and you have it to a
T. Her striped strawberry silk frock, piped
with black velvet, recalled the party-frocks of our
mothers’ young days (when a white “French
merino,” piped with black velvet, was considered
particularly “chaste.”) The flat sleeves, without
an acorn of shoulder-puff, were insertioned with
narrow black lace. Next to her violin, LJrao’s
salient point is her neck, which amusingly plump
member she certainly doesn’t hide under a bushel.
Encroaching tresses were combed up out of the
way, and the gown collar dipping to a point of
three inches or so at the back allowed a roll of fat
to bulge temptingly above the edges of this V.
Though Mdme. Sapio affected a similar spinal-
cord ventilation, her anatomy did not run so con-
spicuously to natural padding, and was moreover
partially shaded by a feather boa. Unlike her
colleague she was fashionably fixed in black with
picture hat en suite, and looked very imposing.
Her husband comprised the suite, but lie made no
pretence of enjoying himself. His curled-up
moustache-ends give him a fictitiously smiling ex-
pression when, in reality, his countenance is
Sphinx-like.
A prominent devotee was a diminutive matron,
who affects drawing-room elocution and undress,
and is an edition de luxe of the little maid in “ We
are Seven !” She looked so innocent, gazing trust-
fully up at the Goddesses, that it was hard to
realise her the mother of a well-grown urchin. A
small boy, who possesses a parent with short curly
hair, flop hat, blouse frock and knack of infantile
expression twice as artless as his own, must find
her a hard nut to crack.
Mdme, Dejardin wore one of the (numerous
black gowns at the Salon, with a Farisiany bonnet,
consisting chiefly of aigrettes. Marie Elster sported
plenty of cuff and collar, and Hose Blayney, who,
by the way, sang nicely, showed up well in a rose-
trimmed hat.
I hear that the Werribee bride looked her level
best on wedding-day. Probably the conscious-
ness of her great pecuniary renunciation
spiritualised her expression, though, after all, she
was to get the fortune for marrying a title, rather
than for not marrying a commoner. The condi-
tion of the legacy was the capture of a lord, and,
if such was not forthcoming, the dismissal of
plain Jones or Brown wouldn’t have mended
matters.
Society journals dwelt long and lovingly on the
chronicles of this toney wedding, going into
details almost ad nauseam. One read in the
“bridesmaids’ list,’’ “Miss Clarke, daughter of
Mr, and Mrs. Rupert Clarke. ” Surely to good-
ness the fact of two parents goes without saying.
E. Jerome Dyer, editor of “Victoria and its
Resources,” was married last week to Blanche
Lansley, one of Melbourne’s prettiest girls and a
vocalist of exceptional ability.

Tattersall's £100,000 Melbourne Cup
Consultation.
Numerous enquiries have been made as to the
manner in which the drawing for this sweep is
conducted. The plan is this. Marbles numbered
from 1 to 100,000 are placed in a monster barrel
fitted up inside like a churn, the marbles being
subjected to a thorough mixing process. The
names of the horses entered for the race are also
printed on marbles, which are placed in a smaller
barrel. One gentleman is then selected by those
present to draw the horses, while another takes
charge of the barrel containing the numbers, and
the drawing then proceeds. Usually the cash
prizes are dealt with first, the marbles being re-
turned to the barrel after each batch of prizes is
drawn, so that every ticket-holder has several
distinct chances of obtaining different prizes.
The marbles are pulled from the barrel one by one
by gentlemen selected by those present. Then
comes the drawing of the horses. The gentlemen
in charge of the large and small barrel draw each
a marble simultaneously, one with a number and
one with the name of a horse. Names and num-
bers are duly entered by a staff of clerks taken
from the gentlemen present, and the drawing
proceeds until the list of horses is exhausted.
When all is completed, the lists are duly com
pared, checked, signed, and countersigned bj
those assisting ; so that there is no chance of a
mistake being passed over. The drawings now
take place in Brisbane, in the presence of press
representatives and citizens of repute, and every
detail is carried out in the fairest manner possible.
Applications for this year’s big sweep are rapidly
coming in.
The arduous labors of Mr. Tom Furse, the
secretary of Sydney Eight-hour Committee, are
drawing to a close, and on Monday his exertions
will be put to the test. Nothing that could be done
to make this year’s Carnival of Labor as great a
success as, or even greater than, its predecessors
has been left undone. Mr. Furse has worked
night and day for the past three months, and if
anything has been overlooked it won’t be the fault
of this energetic Australian. The benefits
possible of attainment by the purchase of a frugal
eighteen-penny (or even a shilling) ticket are fully
set forth in our business columns.
Wool and Produce Reports.
M‘Burney, Wallis & Co. report:—The wool-sale ol
this week consisted of small catalogues only. Prices
were equal to last auctions. We refrained from offering
wool because it is apparent that the attempt to open this
wool season’s sales in September has been the dismal
failure which has marked similar attempts made prior to
actual commencement of previous seasons. It is un-
doubtedly impossible to sell wool before it is received
|nto store, and even supposing a fair amount to be housed
in Sydney in September, to offer in that month is to place
a producer’s clip before only half the number of buyers as
compared with the purchasing power always present at
our October sales.
The London wool sales have opened with prices up to
best rates of last series for greasy merino, and a 5 per
cent, rise in crossbred. We did not expect to hear of a big
increase in value over the water, because we believe our
own sales, which open on the Bth of next month, will be
the best guide as to the treatment the wool-growers of
our colony are to receive at the hands of buyers in the
ensuing season. Our market has now assumed the proud
position of independence, and must speak for itself. We
have been holding over the clips entrusted to our care,
and early in October will bring forward a large and
attractive catalogue of new wool, which will have the
advantage of being placed before the full buying strength
of the world.
Sydney, Sept. 21st, 1894.
The New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency
Co., Limited, report;—The news from the London wool
sales, which commenced on 18th inst., has been a little
disappointing, less ground than was anticipated having
been gained. So far the advance in prices has been con
fined principally to crossbred wools and scoured sorts ol
merino, and amounts only to about 6 per cent., whereas,
from the tenor of advices received just prior to the com-
mencement of the auctions, it appeared almost certain
that prices would open at an advance of from 5 to 10 per
cent, all round. In this market a fair assortment of new
clip wools—about 4000 bales altogether—has been placed
before the trade during the past week, and, judging by
the business transacted, prices now current here are
about Jd. per lb. below those ruling at a corresponding
time last year for average greasy merino fleece, while
as compared with twelve months ago pieces and other
skirtings, which sold so remarkably well last season, are a
full id. per lb. cheaper. Now that the new clip is being
opened up fully, it is found that the wools vary a good deal
in point of condition, but from the growers’ point of view
the clip, as a whole, will no doubt prove a profitable one;
as there is, in most instances, plenty of yolk in the staple
which, while reducing the value per lb., increases the
weight sold. If anything, the early’ shorn wools appear to
be somewhat heavier in condition than were the first in-
stalments of the preceding clip. This company will hold
their next sale on October 8, when a catalogue of about
2500 biles will be submitted.

SPORTING. NOTIONS.
Of the three-year-olds that performed at the
rtoent Randwick meeting Cobbity may eventually prove
the best, and, next to Moorite, his chance is to be preferred
for the V. 8.0. Derby to that of any other candidate.
» ♦ * *
Kelso must fancy The Captain (still on the big
side) for Caulfield Cup, or he wouldn’t have withdrawn
him from Waverley Handicap—a gift for him at 7st. 41b.
» * * *
When owners pull out their two-year-olds
against horses their seniors in age, it indicates confidence
in the quality of the youngsters’ trials. The only A.J.C.
Derby horse that came well through such an ordeal last
season was Moorite, who, giving a year, beat the brilliant
Hova at even weights, just prior to the latter’s New-
market Handicap victory. This stamps him as an all-
right-one, and as he is said to have made commensurate
improvement from a two to a three year old, his Fleming-
ton chances look rosy.
• * • «
In the writer’s opinion, Tridentine, Ruenalf,
Ohesterman and Ronda are the most dangerous quartette
in Melb. Cup.
* * * «
Gaillardia, the flying daughter of Trenton, will
be the Caulfield Cup mount of clever Chris. Moore, who
may thus get level with his cruel luck (Tim Swiveller’s
disqualification) last year.
♦ * * «
The backers of Lady Trenton and Paris sup-
ported these mokes for tons of money in the face of the
«• chucked-in ” Projectile. This splendidly-bred horse
(Chester-Peroussion) was raced off his legs as a two-year-
old, and gave but one glimpse of his true three-year-old
form; but rest has done him worlds of good, and his easy
Metrop. victory landed his owners from £15,000 to £20,000
•n bets.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
On the Metrop. running, Oxide, Paris, Nightin-
gale, Lady Trenton and Loyalty should be close together
when Melb. Cup is run ; but though a probable winner
■nay not be among the quintette, the writer does predict
that Loyalty will be nearer than any of the other four.
» * * *
If the Melb. Cup is to go to the Sydney side of
ziorder, ’twill be by the agency of Ruenalf.
♦ * ♦ •
Reversals of form and the downing of favorites
bad, no doubt, much to do with the small attendance at
Randwick on the concluding day, Saturday last, of the
A.J.C. Meeting. The day opened disastrously for backers
by Hopscotch winning the High Weight Handicap, for
which Milkmaid was heavily backed down to 3 to 1.
Reconstruction, at 8 to 1, won the Members’ Handicap
from the much backed Cobbitty and Atlas; and then
Mutiny put backers in a bit better humor by winning the
Second Steeplechase, tor which He started an equal
favourite with Grandwing. Oxide was picked upon as
the most likely for the Waverley Handicap, but he faded
to run into a place, and the winner turned up in Nightin-
gale. Patron started an even-money favorite for the
Randwick Plate, but when it came to finishing Chester-
man wore him down and won by four lengths. What
money the public had left they planked on Bungehah for
the Final Handicap, but it was not to be, as the places
were filled by Carnation, Loyalty and Albercore, although
it was not till the last 50 yards that Bungebah’s backers
were put out of their misery.
* * * *
Shotbolt (Newminster —Brunette) is getting on
nicely at Ballarat. This horse will run well in whichever
race Scobie advises he should be spun. His probable
journey—Caulfield Cup.
* * * *
Ohesterman, in the Caulfield at fist. 71b., and in
the Melbourne Cup at 6st. 81b., is as likely a double as we
have had for many a day.
• * * *
The result of the A.J.C. Meeting would indi-
cate that age has told on the three cracks, Marvel, Paris
and Bungebah, Oxide, too, has lost his old-time dash.
* * * *
Many hold that the thoroughbred is as good as
ever he’ll be at three years, and they want the w.f.a. scale
revised accordingly. Certainly, Loyalty at the A.J.C.
showed no improvement on his running of last year.
* * » «
Many jockeys now think thatßill ” Long’s
proposal re fees would, if carried, benefit them. For
instance, the winning rider in the Epsom, supposing the
prize to be £lOOO, would then get £55, whereas he is now
entitled only to £5.
• * » *
One prominent Sydney penciller volunteered to
act as private detective against cash-bettors in the pad-
dock, during the A.J.C. meeting. Cash-betting, despite
the objections of certain interested people, is preferable
to the other system on the principle of being the smaller
of two evils.
• * * *
Pedestrianism is still the same old game. At
the Carrington re-opening one ped. was disqualified for
six months, while several other cronks were fortunate
enough to pass unnoticed.
'* * » *
At the Carrington (Sydney) re-optening Samuels,
the old-time aboriginal champion runner, finished last in
his heat. His day is past. Too much lemonade.
* * * *
Creedon displays pluck, at any rate, in chal-
lenging Fitzsimmons. But he is mistaken in asserting
that Fitz. cannot fight strong at list. 41b.
* * * *
On July 28, Fitzsimmons added Kellar to his
ast of victims. Fitz. allowed Kellar to make play in the
first round, but no sooner was time called for the second
than Fitz. strode over to his opponent’s corner and smote
him on “ the point” with the right. It took three doctors
15min. to bring Mr. Kellar round.
* ♦ ♦ *
George Giffen says his real reason for deserting
the Norwoods was because, among other outrages, they
even refused to elect him an Association delegate, thus
shutting him out of the Cricket Council. Giffen certainly
has much to complain of, considering what he has done
f or S.A. cricket.
* * * •
Walter Giffen follows big brother George in
deserting Norwood (S.A.) cricket club.
• * * ♦
Brookwell, the young Surrey batsman and
bowler, and Ward, the Lancashire “sticker,” will have
most to do with the success or failure of Stoddart’s
Eleven. If they “ come off ” pretty consistently the
Britishers should get through the season without any
crushing defeats.
* ♦ ♦ *
We shall soon see how much reason there was
in Blackham’s sneerful remark that if Richardson were
bowling on Australian wickets he would have the leather
knocked off the ball.
» * * *
Peel is said to have netted .£l7OO clear by his
benefit cnckel-match, the other week, so the left-handed
bowler, with his Australian tour engagement to carry him
over the coming English winter, promises to be a com-
fortable capitalist before he retires from business.
* • » ♦
Isaac, like so many bearing his ancient name, is
but a flat-catcher.
(Continued on page 18.)
Robert Buchanan, poet, playwright, novelist,
and philosopher, owned up in his recent bankruptcy
schedule to having lost £l2OO by backing horses. A
serious Scotch bard fossicking after something “straight
from the stable ”is a painful subject for reflection. One
would scarcely be more shocked by the discovery that
Swinburne had just advised Ruskin to have a bit on Billy-
goat for the Welter Handicap if Snooks weighs out for the
mount.
“Not One”; “I would like The Bulletin
contributor to give a little detail over his slapdash state-
ment that the veteran bookmaker almost invariably goes
wrong. With a pretty wide knowledge of the craft, Ido
no* hear of one unrespectable man who has not been
regarded as such all his life. On the other hand, please
let me cite such plain individuals as the Thompsons,
Joseph, Goyder, Gienister, and a score of others that
rise to the lips ; some lucky, some not, but all cashy."

Dear Bulletin, —The scarcity of old book-
makers is accounted for by the nature of their calling.
There is not one bookmaker in a thousand who, even
though he lead an abstemious life, can endure the mental
excitement of six races a day, day after day and week
after week, for a long stretch of years. The men,
especially the Christians among them, break down in
their minds as the horses ultimately go cronk in their
legs. It is a common expression at London betting clubs,
“ Poor So-and-so has broken down.” Bookmakers become
paralysed pretty freely. A lot of them die insane. Davis,
the first layer of odds to be called a leviathan, lived 20
years, or more, of lonely life in his house at Brighton
(Eng.), refusing to see any of his old friends. His legs
were completely paralysed. His mind was diseased. He
had broken down.— Punter.
• * • *
Nothing more forcibly illustrates the extent of
Melbourne’s depression than the presence of a book-
maker’s bag, name and all, in a pawnshop window.
♦ • ♦ »
Goddard is to meet Quigley, October 22, In his
ancient form, the Barrier man should win easily, but he is
comparatively old and his recent defeats by Maori Laing
and the Yankee Englishman, E. Smith, are ominous. He
may, however, overpower Quigley in the first few rounds,
and then would want to tackle his old opponent, Mick
Dooley.
* * • •
The residents of Randwick complain of the
stench and want the local graveyard closed. It’s not the
dead men—it’s the stiff horses on the course.
• * • •
The provision in the Tasmanian Tate Act to ex-
tend the use of the machine to regattas and axemen’s con-
tests is distinctly good. What nice cronk running there
would be if it were applied to pedestrianism 1 No one
would win unless he was paying a good div.

Dear Bulletin, —How does this strike you?
Adelaide had a race-meeting the other Saturday, and the
totalisator investments for six races were:—
No. 1 .. .. £946 0 0
„ 2 .. .. 1569 10 0
„ 3 .. „ 1935 10 0
„ 4 ~ .. 1989 0 0
„ 5 .. .. 1583 0 0
„ 6 .. ... 1882 0 0
Total, £9905 0 0
Commission, £990 10s.; Average investment, £1650.
Now, the machine, unlike the bookmaker, is on a strictly
cash basis. It takes no risks and makes no bad debts.
Suppose the racing public of Sydney start a four days’
meeting, six races each day, totalisator legalised. If
the available betting capital to start with is, say,
£BOOO, the machine takes 10 per cent, of it six
times a day, and the whole of the money would
be practically all gone on the second day—that
is, if they were mugs enough to bet their whole strength.
With an average of, say, £5OOO on each race they would
be clean broke some time on the third day. Now, I think
that if the Anti-Betting League wants to suppress
gambling it should get the totalisator in, then we won’t
have any money to bet with. No wonder it is killing
racing in Maoriland. Monte Carlo is not a patch on the
innocent machine. I have had no experience of its work-
ing in any way, but the figures quoted in the Adelaide
papers have given me the nightmare. I wish I were a
totalisator.—Yours everlastingly, Herod.
• • • •
Statesman Buckley on the Gaming Bill in Maori-
land Leg. Council: “I have been astonished to find that
at one of the Maori race-meetings to which a totalisator
peimitwas granted, the ‘plate’ offered was 2s. 6d. and
two boxes of matches!”
♦ • ♦ •
Dear Bulletin, —“Trooper” is right in stating
the absurdity of applying English drill to Australian
Volunteers,” for the very simple reason that the average
“Australian Volunteer ” sits his horse, but does not ride
it. Good riding in Australia is generally recognised not
so much by the “ perfect balance of the horse ” as by the
rider’s power of "sticking on.” As regards the “horses
sent from cavalry regiments for no fault except that the
would not keep the ranks this may have happened, but
is certainly the exception, not the rule. The difficulty
generally is to persuade the average horse to leave the
ranks at all; and most assuredly any horse that bolted
“at a charge” and left the ranks, would subject its rider
to an unlimited term of extra riding-school for “awkward
riding.”—Yours, &0., Hussar.
* • * •
Editor Bulletin.— You are wrong in your
theory that “in a warm climate the thorough-
bred is reverting to the Arab.” Which Arab?
The Nejdi Arab, which is regarded as the best,
and cannot be bought for money, is a little weight-carrier
from 14 to 15 hands, with immense bone, and great thick
shoulders and haunches. The next best strain of Arab
blood, that of Jibl Shomer, in Central Arabia, often pro-
duces large and powerful horses up to 16 hands. In
Hadramat, in the extreme south of Arabia, there is
a breed of black horses running over 16 hands. Bruce,
the Abyssinian traveller, who stood 6ft. 6in. in his stock-
ings, and was built in proportion, rode a big Dongola
horse, which was quite up to his enormous weight. I
mvself ride 15 stone, and some years ago I used to ride a
little “Gulf” Arab barely 14 hands, that I bought in
India for 20 rupees—about £2 at the time. He was only
a little mongrel, but he could gallop easily with my
weight, and once carried me 80 miles in a day without
absolute distress. Nearly all the Arab horses that find
their way to India are similar mongrels imported from
Bushir by dealers. The best-looking of them are palmed
off on the unwary, by means of forged pedigrees, as Nejdi
horses. The Nejdi Arab regards these pedigrees with
scorn.—R.
» * » *
Dear Bulletin, —Ladas’ defeat by Throstle, in
the Doncaster St. Leger, spoils Rosebery’s triple crown.
The Town Moor has carried more surprises than any race-
course in existence, and Yorkshiremen all over the
world prefer (from experience) a long shot for the Leger
to a red-hot “certainty." I backed Throstle (by letter),
in England, at 40 to 1, last July, partly because Capt.
Machell (the modern wizard of the English turf) fancied
him, and partly because my youngster suffered from
thrush (the bird is called throstle in Yorkshire) at the time
that Ladas won his Derby. It may interest your many
Yorkshire readers to know that I, a Doncaster man,
backed Caller 'Ou when she beat Kettledrum for the
Leger at 100 to 1, Lord Olifden at 40 to 1, Achievement
(Hermit’s conqueror) at 24 to 1, Apology (supposed to
be dead lame) at 50 to 1, and Dutch Oven at 40 to 1, when
she beat Geheimniss in this race.—Yours, Borisonia.
* ♦ » «
Joe Thompson has quite deserted the English
racecourse for the more placid, if not so congenial, occu-
pation of turf commission-agent. He is quartered at 59
Jermyn-street, and appears to have a good clientele.
His office is quite a rendezvous for Australians. He is
looking very aged, and has changed much during the last
two years, ever since a severe attack of influenza. Brother
Barney is, of course, his right-hand man. The whilom
Adonis of the ring is now as bare of thatch as a billiard-
ball. Joe complains that English “ swells ”do not pay
up as promptly as Australian gentlemen.
♦ • » *
The Bulletin has already noted that pigeon-
shooters use assumed names. Judging from those as-
sumed, competitions of late are chiefly confined to makers’
amateurs, as the bicyclists say. Some specimens:
“Modini,” “Maxim," “Lockwood,” “ Raynor,” &0., &c.
♦ * * »
The Johnsonian Club, Brisbane, which had of
late years become sleepy, has had a rude awakening at
the hands of certain University footballers. In a soft
moment the committee elected the whole ’Varsity team
as hon. members. After a fortnight’s experience the
Johnsonians declared that nothing would induce them to
give honorary membership to another football team.
• « * •
Thanks to Dr. Fiaschi, fencing is likely to soon
become a popular sport in Sydney.
The successful removal of Piles of the
most stubborn description is being effected by
Sharp’s new “Cannalin ” treatment, large quan-
tities of which are being forwarded to sufferers
throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with
advice, from 257 George-street, Sydney.

STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I THOS. MUDIMAN, Master Tailor, of 547 George-street, Sydney, in the Colony of
New South Wales,, do hereby solemnly and sincerely declare as follows -
Whereas I suffered severely from a very troublesome affection of the Nerves, which
affected me so much that I was quite unable to attend to my business. I tried several doctors
and many remedies, but all of no avail. Whereas I. as a last hope, obtained some of Herr
Rassmussbn’s Purely Herbal “Alfaline
Vitality Pills,” which I hereby solemnly and
sincerely declare Completely Cubed Me with-
in a few weeks, and although this is over five
years ago I have remained well ever since.
The Alfaline Pills also cured my wife, and
I make this solemn declaration conscientiously
believing the ,«me to bate™.
Subscribed and declared at Sydney this 23rd
day of November, 1891, before me.
S. T. Whiddon, J.P.
Also witnessed by A. Baly, Esq., a Com-
missioner of the Supreme Court of New South
Wales and Victoria.

A Lady Cured of General Debility.
Herr Rassmussen. March 15th, 1894.
Sir, —I hereby beg to certify that having
been a sufferer from General Debility of the
Nervous System for over seven years, and
having tried without success several of the
leading doctors both in Sydney and Melbourne,
I was advised to try your Alfaline Remedies.
I am thankful I did so, for after a few courses
of the Alfaline Remedies I am now completely
cured, and I shall be most happy to_ recom-
mend your remedies to anyone suffering as I
was. Thanking you for your courtesy and
the uniform patience with which you have
treated me, allow me to say that you can
make what use you may think proper of this.
Wishing you every success, allow me to remain,
yours, CATHERINE HAGERMAN.
As witness : John Hagerman (my husband),
caretaker, Tivoli Theatre, Castlereagh-street),

Rumoured that Russia is negotiating for
the purchase of most of the Portuguese colonies,
including Goa in Hindostan and Timor, East
Indies. If this is so the band will begin to play
shortly.
“ Man,” says J. A. Balfour, the coercionist
politician and philosopher, “is no longer the final
cause of the universe, the heaven-descended heir
of all the ages. His very existence is an accident, his
history a brief and discreditable episode in the life
of one of the meanest of the planets.”
St. Stead, in last Review, says that since
the publication of “Maiden Tribute” lots of
women bothered about sex questions have looked
upon him as a kind of glorified father confessor.
This is the most joyous position that a dirty saint
can possibly wish for. Only such a one is privi-
leged to say, as Stead does in his Chicago book,
that “Few people realise that a young and pretty
woman can make more money for a time, by what
may be called a discriminate sale of her person,
than the ablest woman in America can make at
the same age in any other profession.”
The Vic. railway revenue this year is on
an average .£5OOO a month below that of ’93, and
’93 was no one knows how many thousands below
’92, and ’92 and ’9l showed the same big drop
compared with their predecessors. Yet the last
three budgets have been based on the assumption
that Victoria was at last “round the corner.”
What the province wants now is a weeping pessi-
mist for its Treasurer, who will assume that the
province will never get round the corner again,
and frame his budget accordingly. Victoria is
sick of hopeful politicians.
Randolph Bedford hurled his eloquence at
an election-meeting the other night as a free and
independent candidate for a Melb. constituency
that he had never attempted to canvass and where
he knew nobody except himself. Randolph's
vigorous Republicanism was finding him some
friends, when, by a ghastly slip of the tongue—of
course—he proclaimed his devotion to Freetrade,
instead of Protection. That was fatal. He apolo-
gised for having so grossly libelled his fixed prin-
ciples, but the meeting laughed a loud, discordant
laugh. They set him down as a Bloated Mono-
polist with a leaning towards anarchy, and
although he cursed Patterson like a good ’un the
electors couldn’t get away from the fact that he
had written short stories for the A.EGUS. Conse-
quently, Randolph withdrew his candidature, a
much-misunderstood journalist. But a time will
come.
Talmage on the Tarawera (M.L.) eruption :
It seemed as if all the hyenas of rage were snarling at
all the flamingoes of beauty. The lake hissed as with ten
thousand serpents when the hot bombs of the mountain
dropped into it. The malodors of burning iron oxides
and magnesia and chlorine and alumina and sulphur
filled all the regions approximate with suffocation,
Strangulation and asphyxia.
The lurid one then jproceeds to describe an Aus-
tralian sunrise:—
The whole sky first took on a pallor, not sickly, but
healthful, as though there were white wings from
the other side shining through. Then there came corusca-
tions, and deep indigoes, and irradiations, and sadnesses of
color, and unrolling scrolls prophetic of more light, and
sombre and holy gleams and rhapsodies of advancing
day ; and then, banners of victory over the darkness.
Then in this wall of Heaven the gates began to swing
open. It was no sudden swinging back of the panels of
fire. There was no grinding of the gates on the
amethystine hinges ; there was no clang of bolts hurled
back from the Imperial portals, but a slow, gradual and
overpowering movement that made me feel there was
more to come, and I wondered if 1 could endure the ex-
panding vision.
An aggressive Melb. potentate, known to
fame as the architect of his own hat, was the other
morning sitting next the door of the omnibus which
serves his own particular suburb and which was
then full to the regulation number of eleven,
when a stranger got in out of the wet. “ Can you
make room for one, gentlemen?” diffidently
enquired the newcomer. The gentlemen-mostly
middle-aged grandees, who regard the ’bus as
their own especial property—didn’t seem at all
inclined to make room and the potentate, afore-
said, who was reading a book as he sat on the
end of the seat near the door, glowered ferociously,
and looking round for approval said with mock
sweetness : “Perhaps, you would like to take my
seat, sir,” and suiting the action to the word he
stood up. Whereupon the stranger did take his
seat and the architect of his own hat making a
false step, fell out into the mud with great violence.
Then the stranger looking round the ’bus for
approval said, pointing to the potentate, engaged
in straightening out his hat and wiping the mud
off his hinder parts: “Gentlemen, sarcasm is
sometimes its own reward.”
CONSIDER THE LILIES.
“ He did not profess to be a saint, but he had lived for
over 40 years in that district and thought he might fairly
claim that he was as good as most people, and if not
better than the two parsons who got up to speak about
him, he would hang himself.”— The Victorian Speaker at
Brighton (Melb.).
Three cheers for Bent, the Speaker bloke 1
So ’elp me ! he’s a boomer :
He’s game to take the floor, an’ choke
A narsty lyin’ rumour;
He doesn’t want no coat o’ paint
To make him look the whiter,
He don’t pretend to be a saint,
But Gawd knows he’s a fighter.
Tom’s lived in Brighton forty year,
And, as yer know, he’s Speaker,
And when he fills the bloomin’ chair
There’s none looks more uniquer.
Yer ’eard them parson chaps condemn
His morals—they was dreamin’.
If Rent was not as good as them
He’d ’ang hisself, like Deemin’.
It just amounts to this—he’s Bent,
The Mercantile Financer;
Ain’t he the boy for Parlyment?
In course he is, I answer ;
He’s done a ’eap for this here place,
And when I treats him coolly—
Well, smash me with the missin’ mace,
And send the bits to Moule.

HOW A LOT OF MONEY LEAKS OUT.
What does a man do when he finds a hole in the
pocket where he carries his money ? Anybody
can answer that question. He has it sewed up, of
course, directly—and good and strong, too. I
Suppose it is with you just as it is with me. When
1 spend money, even foolishly, I can tell where it
went, and maybe I’ve had some sort of pleasure
out of it. But I do mortally hate to lose money;
lose it out and out, you know, and have no satis-
faction from it, or know how or when it left my
possession.
Well, now, let me show you the worst and biggest
hole any man ever had in his pocket; a hole
that lets the cash leak away like water through a
sieve, a hole that is the hardest in the world to
sew up. A short story will show it best,
“ Drummore South Cottage,
“ Musselburgh, near Edinburgh,
“September 16th, 1891.
“ Gentlemen, —Up to 1885 I was always strong
and healthy. About this time I began to feel bad.
I was tired, languid, dull, and listless, and every-
thing was a burden to me. I had no desire for
company, and what had come over me I could not
make out. My tongue and mouth were dry, and
I had a deal of phlegm on my stomach. The
whites of my eyes next became discolored and my
skin was yellow. I had no appetite, and after
eating I had great pain at my chest and sides, also
across my stomach. After a time the pain settled
in my left side, and my heart would beat and
jump in a manner that alarmed me. By-and-by I
got so weak that I was not able to go about the
house, and I felt that I ought to be in bed. The
pains at my side and stomach became so bad that
I had to remove my clothing (everything seemed
so tight), and I used to press my stomach and
hold my sides to try and ease the pain. Getting
worse, I saw a doctor at Musselburgh, and was
under him for three months, but his medicine
gave me no relief. After this I went to a clever
doctor at Preston Pans who said I was suffering
from indigestion and dyspepsia. He sent to
London for some celebrated medicine which was
packed in small phials. This medicine seemed to
dissolve my food, and I felt easier for a time, but
I gained no strength or real benefit, and after per-
severing with his treatment for six months, I
gave it up and fell into my old state. I next
went to a doctor at Musselburgh, but all his
medicines did me no good. After this I saw
another doctor (that is a fourth doctor), but with
the same result; none of them gave me anything
that reached my complaint. I now lost all faith
in physic, for I had spent a deal of money and
taken so much medicine ‘ that I lost all my teeth
through it ’ and was no better for it. In great
misery I lingered on month after month, always
ailing, when in August of last year (1890), my
husband called at Mr. Jack’s Drug Stores, High-
street, Fisherrow, and told him what my condition
was. Mr. Jack gave him an account of the
wonderful cures he heard of from many of his
customers that had taken a medicine called
Seigel’s Syrup, and strongly recommended him to
bring me a bottle. He did so, and I commenced
taking the Syrup, and I found some relief from
the first bottle, and by the time I had taken four
bottles I was as well and strong as ever I was in
my life, and have since kept in good health. I tell
every one what Seigel’s Syrup has done for me,
‘ I never thought to get better again, and I con-
sider it has saved ray life. I wish others to know
this, and if by publishing this statement it will be
the means of helping others, as it helped me, you
can use this letter as you like. Yours truly
(Signed) Jemima Watson.”
Look back to about the middle of the above
letter and again read what the writer says: “I
had spent a deal of money for medicine.” Yes,
and money she could poorly afford to spare. Ill-
ness and the expense of illness is the great hole in
the pocket that I alluded to. It costs so much,
and what does it give us in return ? Pain, weari-
ness, and misery. There is another consideration
besides. When we are ill we not only have to
bear the increased outgo, but manage to meet
larger demands out of a decreased income. Our
candle is burning at both ends. “Yes,” you say,
“ but how can we keep from falling ill ?” You
cannot always, but in view of the fact that most
illnesses arises from indigestion and dyspepsia, a
timely use of Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup will
prevent it. A few shillings thus invested will
save pounds in money and perhaps months of
wretchedness. Think over the striking points in
Mrs. Watson’s excellent letter and you will think

The editor of a Transvaal paper, out of
curiosity,_ recently measured a few young Boers
standing in front of the Petersburg Court-house.
The list was topped by a strapping fellow aged
19, at 6ft. 61sin., and then came one at 6ft. 4£m.,
five at 6ft. 3m,, four at 6ft. 2in., seven at 6ft. lin.,
and three little ones at the level 6ft. That’s a
better average than even bush Australia can show.
Dear Bulletin, — I am a road-maker em-
ployed by contractors under Govt, on the N.S.W.
South Coast. From Cooma and Monaro to Eden,
and from Eden to Bateman’s Bay, nearly all
contractors keep boarding-houses. A contractor
will not employ you unless you stop at his board-
ing-house. The charge is 15s. to 16s. per week
for tucker only (sleep in your own tent). Prices
for other requisites : tobacco, 6s. per lb. ; bar of
soap, Is. ; matches, 9d. doz., and so on. Contracts
are cut very fine now, and it seems to me all the
profit is made out of board. I can board myself
easily for 7s. or Bs. a week. Why should Ibe
compelled to truck my wages to a Govt, contractor?
Men on piece average about 4s. a day when work-
ing, and day men not much more. Consequence
is, when the contractor’s bill is settled, we take
the road again just about as poor as when we
started the job.—Yours, Roadman.
A late pastor of a very old Melbourne
Anglican Church had some queer parishioners,
amongst them a neighbouring hotelkeeper, who,
though he paid pew-rent and so on, never attended
church. When ill almost unto death, the eldest
son waited upon the Rev. B with a view that
he should visit the dying man and prepare him
for the long journey. The clergyman hesitated.
Said he: “Your father is a peculiar man. No
doubt he subscribes liberally to this church and he
has several times supplied new ropes for the bells,
of which I approve; he also supplies the ringers
with free beer, of which I do not approve, although
I understand the beer is good. But he does not
believe. You know the Scripture says that ‘he
that hath believed and hath fallen away ’ is con-
demned to hell fire.” “But,” interrupted the
son, “my father never did believe, so that that
cannot apply to him.” “Ah, well,” replied the
parson, “ that point might save him, but I don’t
know—anyhow, I’ll see him.” And he did.

The sheep-dog trial at last Corowa,
N.S.W., show, characterised by its sedate sancti-
moniousness and absence of all naughty language,
was a striking contrast to the one of four years
ago. Then, thqse engaged in attending to the
dogs were not particular as to the phrases they
used in addressing them, so, “for the sake of
common respectability,” the committee were com-
pelled to prohibit all strong language in the en-
closure. This was a poser for some of the shep-
herds, whose dogs understood nothing but thunder-
ing curses, but all went well, until one dog, utterly
astonished at the mild and pious tone assumed by
his master, “sat down ” and looked up in his face
in amazement, allowing the sheep to scatter over
the ring. This was too much for the pent-up
feelings of the gentle shepherd, who, turning
savagely round, called out: “Say, judge, can I
call my dog a blanky blank ?” and then, without
waiting for a reply, shook his fist at the dog and
bellowed out : “ Well, you are a blank
blanked blanky , , , fool 1” He did
not win the competition with his unregenerate
beast. All this year’s dogs were saintly animals
and every one possessed a Sunday-school cer-
tificate.

It is commonly reported that the Maoriland
Government will buy up most of the B.N.Z.’simmense dead
weight of landed property, and subdivide it into small
farms. If it does this, a whole lot of big estates will be
broken up, and the ultimate benefit to the people will be
very great. The only serious difficulty is the question of
price. If the estates are bought at their hook value, no
one can say exactly where the Government will be. And
if they are bought at their present market value, it is
hard to say where the bank will be.
* • • •
Until N.S.W. Civil Service Superannuation
Fund is used for insuring the lives of those who contribute
to it—and not merely, as now, for supplying pensions to
civil-servants over 60 years, and compensation to those
retrenched-it will remain a snare and a delusion to a
large body of civil-servants who are compulsorily sub-
scribers to a fund already bankrupt, from which the
greater number of them can never hope to reap any
benefit. The old, crippled mother of the young lady-
teacher, lately killed at a Newcastle railway-station, can-
not claim one cent of the money regularly deducted from
her daughter’s cheque, and paid into this fraudulent fund;
and as an aggravaiion of the hardship in this particular
case, the deceased lady was the mother’s sole support.

The Mutual Life of New York will, it is said,
pay the big sum for which Mr. A. S. Low assured in
Sydney a few days before his death. Deceased had bsen
declined by, at least, one other office, but he, it appears,
had not received formal news of the refusal when, in his
application to the N.Y. Mutual Life, he stated that he
had not been rejected elsewhere.

“It is reported that the trust accounts of pro-
minent Hobart solicitors are in an unsatisfactory con-
dition,” says the Sydney Telegraph’s correspondent.
The accounts of prominent solicitors in general have a
tendency that way. The Bulletin knows one man who
is lying in bed at his hotel because his prominent solicitor
collected his divs. last year and hasn’t accounted for them
yet. He is staying in bed because he reckons that the
-sordid hotelkeeper won’t fire him out in an undressed
state, and he has the bed drawn close up to the window
so that he can look out every few minutes to see if his
money is coming. Yes, the trust accounts of a good
many prominent solicitors are in a very unsatisfactory
condition indeed,

mises will be closed to-morrow to enable us to fake up our

Rev. Dr. Torrance, of St. Kilda, Melb., com-
plains bitterly of the 3d. bit. Consider its possibilities,
reverend sir. With a 3d. bit and moderate knowledge of
the geography of the Insolvency Court, a Melbourne man
can now pay six or twelve pounds’ worth of debts. We
know one gentleman whose Sunday sprat represented
£24, and he gave it ungrudgingly.

off split sovereigns with a bogus backing. Each sov.
makes two, but the labor seems worth the difference.
The cloven ’oof again.
• * • •
It would appear that two banks are fighting
one another indirectly for the butter-export trade of one
province. One man has spent £200,000 on developing the
trade locally, and the rival bank seeks (through its shipping
connection) to pluck the fruit off the developer’s enter-
prise, by cutting off the developer’s middleman profit and
dealing directly between the Victorian producer and
British consumer.

The firm of M‘Lean, Bros, and Rigg (headed by
William M‘Lean, who was “General” Booth’s Melb.
host, and is now insolvent after a plucky struggle to keep
afloat) was an offshoot by the employes of M'Ewan & Co.,
and naturally won its success in the face of hitter hostility.
Like Speight’s, M‘Lean’s downfall is attributable to the
cobbler not sticking to his last.

The Maoriland Government’s 4 per cent, consols
are to be sold to local residents only, and the interest will
be paid in the province and nowhere else. This is in-
tended to prevent that übiquitous speculator, J. Bull, from
buying up the securities and taking the interest out of
the country, and it the precautions are sufficiently
Stringent it will keep £40,000 per annum in Maoriland
which would otherwise have gone to London and come
back never again.

The MTlwraith land-grant railway scheme is
moving once more. That reliable old company-monger
and guinea-pig, Major-General Feilding, is coming out
with proposals from a British syndicate, and the great
design of giving away half Queensland to English capita-
lists to induce them to build railway’s, which will be
owned in England, may come to something even yet.

Dear Bulletin,—Wages in the Q. sugar dis-
tricts. The other day a Bundaberg plantation manager
offered a billet on a selection of his to a decent young
fellow who had come to gain cane-growing experience.
Terras, six months’ agreement, employer to find
with lea, flour, sugar, and beef, and a black gin to cook
for him—this to be full remuneration for his services;
working hours, six to six, and four on Saturdays. I know
of mills where the hands are working amid steam nearly
seventy hours a week for £1 and food hardly fit for a
Chinaman. And what is it all for?-Yours, The Grafter.
Childers, Q., 3rd Sept., ’94.
* * • •
The B.H. mine-owners, after the strike, two
years ago, instituted a comprehensive system of boycott
under which it was decreed, amongst other things, that
the local weekly smelting-yields, formerly available at the
mine offices, should be withheld from the local labor
organ. The boycotted journal at once arranged to have
the yields wired from Adelaide, and published them as
usual, to the no small disgust of the mines people. All
the mines save the Proprietary have now suspended local
smelting, but the boycott is still in force, and the journal
is still under the necessity of procuring the yields of the
mine, which are published as local news, from Adelaide.

The great Manchester (Eng.) ship-canal
threatens to turn out a ruinous white elephant. The
corporation of Manchester has guaranteed the interc st on
the debentures, and the prospects are that the city will
be struck with a tax of Is. 7£d. in the £ on all rentals, in
addition to the existing taxation, to meet the liability 1
• • * *
Some legal questions arose the other day con-
cerning the will of Walter Benny, of Carlton (Melb.), who
died a few years ago while there was still “confidence” in
the land. Benny left a large fortune behind him, and
directed that it should be invested in “ shares of any
bank or banking co., or gas co., or bonds or debentures
of any other co., or deposits in or with any bank or bank-
ing co.” It seems strange to reflect that there actually
was a time when sane Victorians left wills like that.
« • • •
The New Chum Railway mine at Bendigo has a
7-foot reef showing gold at a depth of 2840 ft. This is the
deepest gold ever found in Australia, and Bendigo in-
vestors are beginning to hope that their saddle-back for-
mations will go right down to the centre of the earth—or
right down to Tophet, as the case may be. The biggest
theological problem of 19 centuries is still awaiting a
solution, and Bendigo is going to work in the only prac-
tical fashion to settle it.
♦ • • •
The old Consul gold-mine, Grenfell, The Bul-
letin hears, will soon be in full swing again. The mine,
last worked 20 years ago, is said to have a 12ft. reef.
From £BO,OOO to £lOO,OOO was taken out of the Proprietary
claim. Straight shaft 200 ft. deep, and lowest depth
reached about 600 ft. Some old miners think this is still
one of the best shows on Grenfell.

Wyalong jumpers gob no encouragement from
the Warden, who, so far, has “ knocked out ” every case
which came before him. Warden Pinnerty, of Cool-
gardie, has given more encouragement to jumping than
all the other Australian wardens put together, and whether
rightly or wrongly will be known hereafter as the
“jumpers’ friend,” a designation quite as complimentary
as that of the hanging-judge.

Editor Bulletin, — In your issue of Bth Sept., I
noticed a par as follows: “Another life is said to have
been given to Baker’s Creek mine by a recent discovery.
What was thought to be a blank piece of ground is found
to contain the lode, and a great quantity of backs are
now ready for working—that is the story, anyhow." In
re this, I should like to ask the company why they opened
a London office lately, and if it is a fact that fully a
quarter of the shares are now held in London? Why,
then, this unloading? I should like the shareholders
residing away from the field to know what manner of life
this celebrated mine is likely to have in the future. This
new find is nothing more nor less than the old run of gold
that was lost through not working past the pinch caused by
the hard bar which exists between the Nos. 3 and 7 levels.
In No. 8 level the co. has had kind, easy country to work,
thus showing that they are only now getting into settled
country, being between 400 and 500 ft. below the bed of
the creek. In this level they met with the “ other life ”
(so-called) 200 ft. from their S.E. boundary dipping fast
away from them towards the No. 1 Extended property.
Fact is. Baker’s Creek mine has only had the cap of this
wonderful shoot of gold, which they are losing fast, and
upon which their divs. are said to depend. “The great
quantity of backs” won’t last 12 months if all is true wo
hear, because of the depth and sharp dip of the shoot (it
having dipped 75ft. in the 100 between Nos. 7 and 8 levels),
and the nearness of the boundary when first met with.
“The other life” rather belongs to the other claim, as
should be proved in a short time. The Baker’s O. mine
would have had a much longer lease of life if she had
been worked economically from the start, as there isn’t
another mining township in N.S.W. with such facilities
as Hillgrove for aheap motive power. The Fails are
1500 ft. deep, and with a battery at the bottom and a dam
on top the crushing could have been done for about a
sixth of what it has cost, leaving out the cost of engines
and boilers, and the wear and tear, to say nothing of the
staff of engineers, mechanics and laborers that the.un-
necessary machinery necessitates. —Yours, Miner, Hill-
grove, Sept. 11, 1894.

Dear Bulletin, —Hav6 you ever noticed
a water-color picture (No. 306 in the_ catalogue) in
the water-color court of N.S.W. National Gallery?
It is entitled “A Venetian Peasant,” by L.
Da Rios. How did this shameless peasant
succeed in forcing her way into the gallery and
escaping the Social Purity Society’s searching
eye (I presume “ L. Hop.” has warrant for draw-
ing them with only one searching eye)? Are we
to assume that this is the average condition of a
Venetian peasant of the female sex ? Or are all
Venetian female peasants similarly situated ? If
this is the chronic condition of the Venetian
peasantry, then Venice must be a very populous
city. Judging by the absence ot any sign of a
ring on the third finger of this brazen creature’s
left hand, I should say that the Venetian
peasantry do not give the registrar of marriiages
much trouble. I saw several elderly mar-
ried ladies looking very anxiously at this
picture, muttering “ Poor thing i” and, on the
whole, one finds oneself thinking of godfathers and
godmothers, and looking eagerly for Sairey Gamp
in the middle distance. What can the trustees
have been doing to permit this distinctly immoral
drawingtoenterN.S. Wales National Gallery? And
where is the Social Purity Society? Surely its
members could spare a day now and then from
the divorce-court to look this shameless Venetian
patient up, and see that she is either removed
from the walls, for about a month, or provided
with proper medical: attendance. At at events,
young people under 50 or so should not be per-
mitted to see this singular example of the state of
morals in the Gity of Venice.^—Yours, Social
Pukitt.
Writes the National”: “Magazine-news-
papers are the best to advertise in. An advertisement
which is not read does no good, and most daily papers are
only half read before they make way for their successors.
The paper that is read from end to end,” like The Bulle-
tin, “is the sheet-anchor of profitable advertising.” A
good, original weekly, not a hideous jumble of reprinted
stale news spiced with observations about horse-colio and
turnip-diseases, is the best medium. Every man knows
that he skims over a daily, at breakfast or on a tram-car.
He never reads it through. But his favorite weekly is
mentally laid aside lor an hour of leisure, and then he
reads it, and reads it right through. The Bulletin cir-
culates everywhere in Australasia, and even sells 300
copies weekly in South Africa. Let the business man
personally observe, or ask his traveller or his travelling
friend to keep his eyes open and say which paper is the
most widely-circu'ated in this continent. There can be
but one answer- The Bulletin.

The Price of a Kiss .
(FOR THE BULLETIN)
Where the ranges dip down to the plain at their
base,
In the lap of the gully lies “ Tressider’s Place,”
And the danoers are footing it merry and bright
For the honour of Kitty, his daughter, to-night.
With a clatter of hoofs and a jingle of belts
The troopers ride up and the merriment melts,
And men stand aghast, who were laughing before,
At the glitter of steel, as they crowd to the door.
Tom Govan, long-hunted, is captured at last,
And the days of his riding and raiding are past;
They bring him, a prisoner, half-ended their task,
And to rest there the night is the favour they ask.
In the stable they lash him to post and to ring.
For the strength of his arm is a marvellous thing,
Then they join in the dance and the night wanes
apace,
And there's laughing and loving at “ Tressider’s
Place.”
But Kitty creeps out and stands weeping apart,
For the love of Tom Govan that lies at her heart.
For in good and in evil, through sin and through
shame.
The love of a woman alone is the same.
But a form is beside her, a voice at her ear,
The voice that of all she least wishes to hear—
’Tis the trooper who first ran her lover to earth,
And whoso love she had treated with scorning and
mirth.
“Kate,” he whispers, “to-night bid your lover
good-bye,
If he leaves here to-morrow Tom Govan will die,
But just smile on me kindly and give me one kiss,
And to prove how I love you I’ll give you—see—
this I”
He holds up his hand and he shows her a key—
One turn in the lock and Tom Govan is free—
He would barter his honour, with traitors claim
kin,
For one smile from the woman he never can win.
She falters a moment, then raises her face.
Puts her hand in his own—“ You may kiss me,”
she says.
“When you’re both far away,” and he toys with
her hair,
“ You might give me a thought—if you’ve any to
spare.”
• * •
There is saddling and mounting at “Tressider’s
Place,”
For of Tom and bis sweetheart no man finds a
trace—
But One lies on the grass, a revolver he grips,
And ’tis the smile he bought, maybe, that’s still
on his lips.
Melb.
ELISE ESPINASSE.
The utter puerility of some Queensland
Separationists was never more strikingly shown
than when certain of the clan gravely requested
Max O’Rell to use his influence to forward the
Separation movement on his return to England.
Blouet gravely assured them that he would lay
the case immediately before the crowned heads of
Europe.
His Hair.
(FOR THE BULLETIN)
The little albino barber was quite a show feature
of that small Maoriland township. He was about
39, unmarried, and without a solitary love-experi-
ence, when one morning a girl stepped into his
saloon.
“ Can you trim my hair, barber?” she asked.
“Not this morn in’. Miss. I’d ’ave t’ shut th’
room ter gents to do it; but if you’ll come ut two-
thirty I’ll be ’appy t’ oblige yer.”
He had all ready when she returned. Envelop-
ing her in a pink wrapper, he let loose the coils of
jet black hair, which, as he lightly shook their
strands apart, fell about his feet in a profusion of
glossy ripples. With a smothered exclamation he
stood back for a moment, his pink expressionless
eyes growing redder-rimmed, his placid features
working with delight in the spectacle before him—■
so unique, so utterly unexpected.
“ I want you to cut a good deal of it off,” she
said ;“itis so heavy. And she sighed wearily.
The usually communicative barber did not reply.
He brushed and combed the wonderful hair, fin-
gering it as though it were of spun glass; then
began to clip.
“You are to take off quite six inches,” she
ordered.
Snick! snick ! the frizzy black fragments piled
into quite a little heap at the albino’s feet, but
still those raven tresses swept the floor.
“Thank you,” she said, when he had lifted and
re-coiled that ebon crown ; “Ishall work with less
sense of oppression now that you have lightened
my load,” and, smiling, she paid her shilling and
went out.
Twice during that summer she came again to
have her hair cut. At the beginning of the cold
weather she came for the fourth time.
“ It’s ’ardly necessary t’ cut any more off,” the
albino objected, caressing the falling hair, which
now hung some inches from the floor.
“Oh, yes! I simply cannot bear the weight;
take off a good length ; it’s too much, such a mass
of hair.” And she settled herself in the big chair,
her small face reflected in the mirror.
The albino saw not the face; his thoughts were
all of that glorious hair —his heart’s first love.
Small and few were the locks he severed ; but
for over an hour he brushed and smoothed, till the
blue-black polish reflected the movement of his
broad, flat-nailed fingers, while she, dozing, noted
not how time passed. Customers were clamoring
for entrance when, with a heavy sigh of regret, he
drew away the wrapper.
“I’m afraid I’ve kept you a long time, but
you’ve taken away my headache,” she said, grate-
fully.
“ ’Eadache! Do you ’ave ’em ?”
“ Almost all the time. I’ll come again soon to
have it charmed away.”
“ I ’ope you will. I’m always free early after-
noons. Yes, I ’ope you will,” repeated the albino,
not looking at her, but speaking to the hair.
She passed out into the street, and met her
headache at the first step on the rude pavement.
The albino, on his knees, carefully gathered
every hair from the sheet he had spread to receive
them as they fell; and placed the gleanings in a
little screw of silver paper upon a shelf among
several similar parcels.
After a month the weather set in cold, bringing
sleet and snow. It was at half-past ten that the
albino, having warmed his small, limp frame
with a pint of beer, turned out hie gas and
went to bed. He had scarcely got to sleep when
a loud banging at his street-door woke him with
a start.
As the banging continued he gob out of bed,
grumbling, and partially opened the door, enquir-
ing, “ ’Oos there ?’’
“ Are you the barber?”
“ ’Oo else d’y’ spose ?”
“ Then you’re to come with me sharp, and
bring along the best case o’ razors you’ve gob.”
“ ’Oos wantin’ me?”
“ Oli, shut up, an’ look alive. D’ye think I’m
goin’ to stand ’ere all night answerin’ questions?”
snarled the man, shivering.
“you’re none too bloomin’ perlite yerself,”
snapped the albino, slamming the door in the face
of the man, who retreated to the waiting buggy,
cursing the weather.
Presently the albino emerged, well wrapped up.
“ Ready ?” asked the man.
“ Ready,”
“ Not forgettin’ the razors?”
“ Not fergettin’ th’ razors.” And neither spoke
again until, after a long drive into the pitch-black,
sodden country, they drew up at a cottage.
The door was opened by a tall man.
“ Come in, my man,” he said, and the albino
entered, blinking at the light.
It was a small room without a fireplace. Two
silent, serious-faced men sab watching him.
“ Remove your web things,” said the tall man,
and when the albino had complied, he continued,
“Now, barber, are your razors sharp?”
“Yes’ir, sharp as razors,” said the albino, his
teeth chattering with cold.
“This is no time for jests,” reproved the tall
man ; “I want you to shave a patient as soon as
you are warm enough to keep a steady hand—a very
steady hand, mind.”
The albino with a low, whistling sigh, drew
forth his ease and examined its contents.
“ Whenever you are ready,” hinted the tall man,
and the three preceded the albino into a larger room
adjoining. The lamp was shaded, bub a bright fire
shed a flickering glow over a bed upon which, moan*
ing at intervals, bub tossing, tossing ever, with
incessant rolling of her pain-racked fevered head,
lay the girl who had come to him in the summer,
the possessor of—his hair.
“This is the patient. You can begin at once,”
said the tall man, as the albino made no sign.
“ I couldn’t do it, sir ! Wot a sacrifice! ” And
all the blood in the albino’s luke-warm body flew
to his face and set his stiff limbs tingling.
“ lb must be done,” said the tall man, sternly.
The albino lifted a wisp of her hair, no longer
glossy and soft; tangled, tarnished, harsh to the
touch of his slow-moving fingers.
They attempted to hold her still, bub no efforts
could quiet her. Anguished at the sight of that
black, dishevelled mass the albino said ;
“ If I’d a brush I think I’d calm ’er down.”
They handed him one, which he drew slowly
and regularly from her forehead to the furthest
point of hair he could reach. At the end of an
hour she lay still.
He clipped every lock, laying each to each as he
relinquished them, until you could have supposed
them severed at one cub.
The shaving was more difficult: the light was
poor.
Bub after two hours there remained a bald head
with a little fringe of black round the face, where
a narrow line of hair still grew.
The albino gathered his tools whispering, “ I
’sposo I’d better take this ’ere ’’-indioatim,
hair-“ an’ make it up : ’er mother mighUik
m^ YeB ’ yes ’ j,,sb as you Piease,” said the til
. “ Pore young thing,” went on the albino wind
ing the hair into a neat coil ’ u '
“It’s very sad,” replied the tall man; “nunil
teacher, teaching all day, studying all night to„
much for mortal woman-the result—Look
mg towards the bed, then, as if struck by some
thing he saw there, he stepped swiftly to the eirvl
side. R 18
“ Barber, you have left a fringe of hair !”
“ Yes’ir, I thought they’d like it, in—in case of
anythin’ ’appenin’—she’d not look so-so Wid
like.” u
“I understand.” And the tall man bowed hi«
head. 18
Two days later something did happen.

The albino wove the hair into a beautiful
plait, secured at each end by a buckle cleverly
made from its own strands. r
Rut her mother never got it.
When he came to the end at last, after six
weeks of loving toil, and was about to send it
away, something snapped at his heart,
He tried to weep ; not a tear. Then he rose and
? astl an enquiring eye upon the beams which
braced his roof.

horror of the thing is that a large number of

“THE EAST of SXX”:

—BY—
ERNEST r AVENG,
With prefatory note by
JRolf JBoldrewood,
PRICE : : Is.
Posted to any Australian
Address , Is. 3d.
“ The volume takes its name from one of the sketches, ‘ The Last of Six,’ though in point of merit they all stand pretty well on a ] ar. The
author, who is familiar with life in Northern Queensland, writes with vigour about the doings of the whites and the natives in these faraway
parts. A strong vein of mysticism runs through his narratives, and there are ghosts in almost every chapter, and for those who are fond of
realistic work the volume will prove attractive reading ” —S.M. Herald, July 8, 181)3.
“ No writer has given the world a more vivid description of Australia and its wilderness than Ernest Favenc, and the secret of this is that
he has, by many years’ experience of travel and bush life, acquired a knowledge of this continent which is possessed by scarcely any other writer.
He, too, possesses great descriptive powers. Whatever he puts pen to is certain to be good. In the stories under notice he has given us some of
his best work. That they will be widely read is certain.”— Sydney Mail, July 8, 1893.
“ They present with force and vividness, as well as ■with the accuracy arising from long contact and keen observation, striking pictures of
parts of Australia unfamiliar to most of its inhabitants ; they tell, sometimes with remarkable power, of incidents such as happen in wild places
when human hearts are stirred by strong passions, and of weird imaginings that move the minds of lonely men. ‘ The Last of Six ’is a terrible
tale of cannibalism, treachery, and revenge upon the dead, which in other hands might be gross and hideous, but which Mr. Favenc makes of
thrilling interest. ‘ The Haunt of the •) inkarras suggests Rider Haggard and his imitators to some extent, but is itself relieved from the
suspicion of imitation by faithful local colour and a grotesquerie of conception, in which Mr. Favenc here and there approaches Foe. This
characteristic is especia ly noticeable in ‘ Spirit Led, which the great American might have written. ‘A Cup of Cold Water is, perhaps, the
best tale in a collection that should be widely read, and which forms a worthy addition to purely Australian literature. ’’—Daily Telegraph
July 15, 1893.
“‘The Last of Six ;’and other stories. By Ernest Favenc. (Sydney: The Bulletin Newspaper Co) This is a collection of short bub
graphic sketches by Mr. Favenc, the well-known explorer, who has a rare collection of Australian legendary lore. All the stories here are good,
and we can heartily endorse the recommendatory notice by Rolf Boldrewood prefixed to this collection. The present issue forms the third of The:
Bulletin scries of Australian reminiscences.”— Australian Star, July 15, 1893.
